PREFACE 
 
 
English title: Sustainability & inclusiveness in the future of the entertainment industry 
Master Thesis in M.Sc. Design & Innovation 
Technical University of Denmark 
31.08.2020 - 21.02.2021 
30 ECTS Points 
154 pages (221 with appendix) 
Supervisor: 
Jaap Daalhuizen, Assistant Professor, Management Engineering 
Collaborative partner: 
Roberto Flore, FoodLab Manager 
This master thesis documents an innovation project carried out at the Technical University of 
Denmark (DTU) in the department of Management Engineering. 
The project was conducted by Carl Emmerik Rohde, Lars Jørgen Riisdal and Máté Gulyás  
The project was initiated August 2020 and ended February 2021. 
The thesis displays the reframing of the entertainment industry, using the Vision in Design and 
Designing for Society methods in developing an innovative concept.  
 
Carl Emmerik Rohde, s152313 
MSc.Eng. Design & Innovation 
DTU Management Engineering 
 
Lars Jørgen Riisdal, s181308 
MSc.Eng. Design & Innovation 
DTU Management Engineering  
 
Máté Gulyás, s182584 
MSc.Eng. Design & Innovation 
DTU Management Engineering 
 
 
 
==PAGE:3==
 
3 
 
Abstract  
 
Some might argue that partying and nightlife do not serve a higher purpose and are only for the young 
generation. However, as designers and entertainment lovers, we - the authors of this thesis - have a 
different point of view. For us, entertainment is a reflection of society’s desires, and the heritage of 
thousands of years of human evolution, which is why we chose it to be our master thesis domain. 
Using the methods of Vision in Product Design (ViP) and Social Impact Design (SID) from Tromp & 
Hekkert, we investigated future possibilities in the domain of city entertainment by 2031 and decided 
to innovate within this domain. Through our research, we identified the trend that people are looking 
for meaningful substance in their life. We also identified a decline in quality and sense of meaning in 
the city’s so-called third places (frequented between private life and work), partially due to less mindful 
use of technology. Until now, few seem to be concerned about how entertainment is consumed in 
society, and how it contributes to the individual’s social life and mental health. Therefore, we chose 
this to be the scope of our research, in a call for more meaningful, diverse, inclusive, and socially 
sustainable entertainment in cities.  
An ambitious concept was developed to answer this call, one aimed at changing people’s lives through 
more meaningful entertainment, leading to positive societal change. The social relevance of the design 
proposal was then assessed by domain experts, including event organizers from the Burning Man 
Network. The feedback was used to refine the concept and generated precious insight for further 
development. The resulting concept is a visionary social space called Heterotopia – a term coined by 
Michel Foucault describing places that are “other” - “perfecting, mirroring, or unsettling the society 
they are embedded in” (Foucault, 2004). Our concept of Heterotopia contains everything an urbanite 
needs to feel fulfilled while actively helping the individual grow and maintain a diverse social circle. At 
the core of Heterotopia is a community of people united in shared values. Heterotopia’s interactions 
and the environment is mindfully engineered with all the knowledge synthesized from our extensive 
research. Carefully designed elements, such as the Event Horizon, The Connection Medallion, and the 
Jungle Room, are aiding the users in overcoming individual concerns and including them in the shared 
vision of Heterotopia – to feel what we have defined as collectively connected: being connected to 
oneself, to others, and to the globalized world.

Chapter 1:  
Introduction 
 
 
 
==PAGE:11==
 
11 
 
1.1 Thesis structure and reading guide 
 
This is a guide on how to read this master thesis. The report is divided into chapters, parts and sub-
sections. Each chapter is the beginning of a new topic. We have 8 chapters for this thesis.  
In Chapter 1 we are introducing the topic of this master thesis and the motivation that sparked the 
development of our innovative design solution. 
Chapter 2 introduces the two methodologies that were extensively used as the main framework for 
our project and for the structure of this report: Vision in Product design (ViP) and Social Implication 
Design (SID), developed by Tromp & Hekkert. 
The first part of the Chapter 3 includes the literature review of the leading research within our domain. 
There are 6 sub-sections in this part. The second part of the chapter includes the list of international 
interviewees who shared their knowledge in the domain.  
In Chapter 4, we follow the ViP-process and reframe city entertainment. This chapter documents our 
design process, starting with a short introductory exercise: Preparation. The main part of our process 
in called Designing and is divided into three parts: Part 1 investigates the Future Context; Part 2 
identifies the Interaction Qualities of the to-be-designed product and Part 3 details out the Concept 
of the new product. 
Chapter 5 presents our solution, Heterotopia. The different parts of the design intervention are 
detailed in this chapter. 
Chapter 6 summarizes the method we used for testing, and the feedback we have received during the 
Evaluation of the Concept. 
Chapter 7 includes our Reflection on the development of this master thesis. It critically analyzes our 
process and the method we used. It is here where we include our suggestions what could be improved 
in the method and the process equally.  
Chapter 8 includes our Discussion and the Conclusion of the project. 
After the last chapter the list of Sources and the Appendix can be found.  
 
==PAGE:12==
 
12 
 
1.2 Motivation and vision 
 
The birth of this project came from a shared frustration, originated in a lifetime of observations.  
The purpose of this study is to make a change in how we consume experiences in cities. Creating a 
vision for 10 years in the future, we are looking at how people in cities could gather and celebrate in a 
way that benefits the individual, the local communities, and society at large.  
Technology has on one side given us more opportunities than ever before, by rapidly transforming the 
way we live life and interact with each other. Simultaneously, as the focus has been more on designing 
for the benefit of the individuals rather than on society as a whole (Tromp & Hekkert, 2018). People 
seem to become more disconnected from each other. Our social life seems shallower and sometimes 
people get confused about what “socializing” really means, as social media platforms have extended 
this meaning to the online world. The physical disconnection between each other has led to an increase 
in loneliness, which is reportedly more dangerous than obesity (Cigna, 2018). If you are alone, you are 
also likely to have fewer opportunities in life (Esteban & Roser, n.d.). By now, loneliness in cities is a 
well-known phenomenon, even though millions of people live side by side (Cacioppo & Patrick, 2008).  
Studies show that we seek to form social groups or “tribes” with like-minded people where we feel 
accepted and respected in the desired group (Tribal Psychology, n.d.). Shared experiences are aiding 
in this, making the group stronger and its members closer to each other. This needs to be taken into 
consideration when we want to redesign the future and propose more sustainable and inclusive forms 
of entertainment.  
Radical inclusion, participation, and radical self-expression are key principles at the event Burning Man, 
which is where we got a lot of inspiration from, especially regarding life-changing events. By observing 
and experiencing the impact of these events ourselves, we felt a need to create more spaces like this 
in cities. Spaces that facilitate deeper connections with ourselves and other human beings, and allow 
continuous self-improvement and reflection, so that we are always able to grow and evolve as human 
beings.  
Our vision is to induce a more mindful and sustainable mindset to people through meaningful 
experiences, hopefully leading to a better society by “creating” better individuals. Conscious that this 
behavioral and value change can’t happen overnight, we are designing a strong vision for 2031, and 
the steps needed on the way. By integrating knowledge from various fields (among others psychology, 
behavioral design, human behavior, and future studies) and alternative event organizers, we are 
making solid foundations for the creation of a new paradigm.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:13==
 
13 
 
 
1.3 Positioning towards COVID-19 
Big changes, harder to predict. The thesis started during the crisis, after an all-time low in new cases 
in Denmark. We are optimistic and believe that the virus will be eradicated during the next few years. 
Nevertheless, the world will not “go back to normal” as it was before the crisis. We believe that the 
crisis will have started new trends, such as a higher demand from employees to work partly from home.  
COVID-19 had a major impact on our research topic, gathering and celebrating in cities. It turned the 
situation upside down, giving us an extra challenge. We were unable to look at the situation in our 
domain during the period of the project. Firstly, very few options were possible, and for our own safety 
and to follow the restrictions, no unnecessary risks were taken. Secondly, the situation was very special 
and wasn’t representative at all of the past or the future.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:14==
 
14 
 
 
Chapter 2:  
Methodology 
 
 
 
==PAGE:15==
 
15 
 
The main methodology used in this paper is called Vision in Design (ViP) which is a design method 
developed by Paul Hekkert and Matthijs van Dijk, both professors at the Delft University of Technology. 
This project aims to successfully utilize the innovative approach of ViP on a socio-cultural 
phenomenon. In addition to the ViP design guide, we used a variant called Social Implication Design 
(SiD) as an add-on. This variant (also developed at the Delft University of Technology) is aimed at 
designing for social impact. 
2.1 Vision in Product Design (ViP) 
 
The method is briefly summarized by the authors in the following way: “ViP is a method that supports 
innovators of any kind to ‘design’ the vision – the raison d'être – underlying their vision. This vision is 
firmly rooted in a deliberately constructed future world. Since the future defines the goal and not the 
means, the method can be applied in innovation processes of any kind. (Hekkert & Van Dijk, 2011)” 
 
Each step has a common starting point, summarized in the following three principles: 
 
1) The designer must look for future possibilities instead of solving problems in the present 
 
2) Products obtain meaning through their interaction (relation) with the user, making ViP 
interaction-centered 
 
3) ViP is context-driven, meaning that the future context where the product will be used is to 
be designed first. Only then, the product will be designed and may require new behaviors 
and experiences. The appropriateness of the interactions is determined by the designed 
context.  
 
There are two parts to the method. The first part can be described as understanding the past and is 
called Deconstruction. The second part is about the future and is simply called Designing. Since the 
vision is created in a future context, the ViP designer spends most of his time in the Designing phase – 
the Deconstruction works as a preparation phase. The picture below illustrates the steps of the 
process. 
==PAGE:16==
 
16 
 
 
Image 1: The ViP process (Reframing Studio, n.d.) 
 
Deconstruction 
In the deconstruction phase, the designer prepares mentally for the design phase by gaining a deeper 
understanding of the to-be-designed object. For this, the designer must go through an analysis of a 
freely chosen product -which fits the purpose- on three different levels. These levels are defined by 
the ViP process as the following: product level, interaction level, and context level. 
 
Product level 
In the deconstruction phase, the product level describes the physical attributes of existing products or 
alternatives, e.g., shape, size, characteristic, materials of the product. 
 
Interaction level 
The interaction level describes how the user is using this product and how their relationship can be 
characterized.  
 
Context level 
Finally, the product is examined at the most abstract level, which is the context level. In this part, the 
object is analyzed in the past context when it was created. The idea is to answer what were the general 
trends at that time, how the designer viewed the world, and why the object was designed the way it 
is. 
 
By the end of the deconstructing exercise, the designer gains a deeper understanding of the object and 
the surrounding environment of the object when it was created.  
 
==PAGE:17==
 
17 
 
Designing 
From this phase, the Designing process starts. It goes through the same levels as the deconstruction 
but in the opposite order. It starts abstract by describing the future context, moves on to describing 
the new product-user interaction, and gets more concrete at the end by describing the new design 
intervention. The designing phase has eight steps to guide the designer through the three mentioned 
levels. 
 
Context level 
Step 1: Establishing the domain 
Establishing the domain is usually done with the client who has already specified the field of 
observation. The domain is like lenses to look at a phenomenon where we want to place our 
intervention. Besides establishing the domain, the designer has to set the future time when the design 
intervention will be realized.  
Step 2: Generation of context factors 
The context factors are the “building blocks” of the future the designer is trying to picture. Context 
factors are general laws, observations, beliefs that have a corresponding value to our domain. They 
are value-free and objective. 
Step 3: Structuring the context 
This step is meant to reduce the number and complexity of the context factors so the designer can see 
the underlying principle. Usually, a pattern or storyline emerges from the clustered context factors, 
which will help to establish the vision statement. 
Step 4: Statement definition 
The statement reflects the likely future discovered through the generation and clustering of context 
factors. It represents the designer’s opinion about the future, whether he agrees or disagrees with the 
future. In this stage, the designer takes a position; hence the statement is influenced by the designer’s 
values and beliefs.  
 
Interaction level 
Step 5: Establishing a relationship: designing human-product interactions 
This step focuses on how the object will be used by the user and how their interaction can be described 
in the best way to support the goal stated in the statement.  
 
Product level 
Step 6: Designing product qualities 
After selecting the appropriate product-human interactions, the designer has to find what product 
qualities will be able to fulfill those interactions.  
 
==PAGE:18==
 
18 
 
Step 7: Concept design, or ‘concepting’ 
This is the first step of the process when the designer can decide what kind of product he/she thinks 
would fulfill the goal and act according to the qualities stated above. Usually, it emerges naturally from 
the observations. 
 
Step 8: Design and detailing 
The designing and detailing part is the final step of the ViP method. It carries out a detailed definition 
of the selected object.  
 
The method allows a profound understanding of different aspects of the to-be-designed object. Most 
importantly, it directs the designer’s attention to the future where the object will come to life rather 
than on the present. The process helps to come up with an innovative solution where the innovation 
is rooted in a deep understanding of the future. It is a real innovation of meaning (Verganti, 2017), not 
just an innovative solution. Furthermore, it highly encourages the designer to make its own decisions. 
This empowerment of trusting the skills of the designer allows a better relation to the project. It also 
encourages the designer to make decisions based on “gut feeling,” which is a unique opportunity 
provided by the method. As a designer, it is important to feel confident and powerful in your role, and 
these qualities of ViP surely allow one to feel so.  
 
We chose the ViP method because we have worked with it before, and we found it very useful. In 
contrast to the usual ‘innovation of solution’ where the innovation is mostly incremental to the 
previous product, we want to reimagine ‘the meaning’ of our domain completely.  
2.2 Social Implication Design (SID) 
 
Social Impact Design (SID) is an effect-driven design method aiming to support designers in designing 
the influence of design manifestations on behavior to counteract social issues (Tromp & Hekkert, 
2016). The design process follows a similar framework as ViP. The designer describes the effects of the 
to-be-designed intervention on three levels: the social level (describing the social realm), the 
behavioral level, and the experiential level. It is only after this that the design intervention materializes. 
The image below illustrates the different steps of this holistic process. 
==PAGE:19==
 
19 
 
 
Image 2: The SID process (Tromp & Hekkert, 2016) 
Social level: The social problem that is designed for is approached neutrally in a holistic way. It is 
reframed as a socio-technical “phenomenon” that concerns multiple aspects of society. The chosen 
phenomenon is therefore seen through different lenses, e.g., from economic, health, or cultural 
perspectives. The designer defines the desired social impact of his intervention. 
 
Behavioral level: The designer then defines the behavioral change required to achieve the desired 
social impact, called behavioral goal.  
 
Experiential level: Finally, the concerned individual’s concerns are examined to assure the 
effectiveness of the intervention. Parallelly, the “appropriateness” should be preserved. 
 
Five steps guide the designer through the three levels. He/she (re)frames the projects (step 1: 
phenomenon), defines the desired social effect (step 2: social statement), focuses on a particular 
behavior to reach this effect (step 3: behavior), considers what type of influence is most appropriate 
and effective (step 4: strategy) and designs the concept (step 5: design proposal) (Tromp & Hekkert, 
2016). 
We chose to integrate SID because of the tools it provided to do a project with a social impact.  While 
the method wasn’t followed step by step, aspects of it were integrated into the thesis. Theories and 
tools described in Designing for Society (Tromp. N, Hekkert, P), such as social dilemma theory and 
behavioral goal, were used in the project. An overview about what exactly what was used from the 
methodology of Designing for Society (Tromp. N, Hekkert, P) can be found in Appendix G.1.

Chapter 3:  
Literature review & Interviews 
 
 
 
==PAGE:21==
 
21 
 
3.1 Literature review 
 
Introduction 
The purpose of this literature review is to analyze and present the existing knowledge in different 
fields, that are important to cover when talking about the future of city entertainment.  
We will first talk about social spaces – in this part we will elaborate on the concept of third place, being 
the place where people go between home (the first place) and work (the second place) to exchange 
ideas, socialize or simply spend leisure time. We will then introduce you to social sustainability, the 
least defined and least understood aspect of sustainability. It is this aspect of sustainability that we will 
consider in our project. As we aim for raising the general life quality of urbanites, we will have a section 
about this topic. Finally, we will cover social connections, as well as loneliness (the consequence of the 
lack of these).  
The literature review will form the academic and scientific basis of the project and outline 
opportunities and limitations. 
 
3.1.2 Third Places 
 
When looking at city entertainment, the concept of the third place is essential to understand. It 
describes the places where people spend time between the first place (home) and the second place 
(work), where one can exchange ideas, have a good time, and build relationships (Butler & Diaz, 2016).  
We will first describe the concept of the third place as it was originally described by sociologist Ray 
Oldenburg (1989), who coined the term. We will discuss whether we can talk of current “virtual third 
places.” as some virtual spaces seem to have similar characteristics to Ray Oldenburg’s definition from 
1989. We will then present recent theories stating the existence of a fourth place. 
Definition 
“Third place” is a term coined by sociologist Ray Oldenburg. It defines social spaces, different from 
home (the “first place”) and work (the “second place”). Examples of third places are bars, clubs, 
hairdressers, cafés, churches, and parks. In his influential book, The Great Good Place (1989), Ray 
Oldenburg argues that third places are at the heart of community building, the grassroots for 
democracy, and establishing a sense of place-  
Characteristics of third places 
Third places are neutral grounds, where anyone can come and go as they please. They are accessible 
and fulfil the needs of their occupants – who are feeling as comfortable as at home and are coming to 
recharge. Third places have a “leveller effect” by putting everyone on the same level, regardless of 
==PAGE:22==
 
22 
 
their social status. Everyone is accepted, and people are usually low profile. The main activity is a 
conversation, and the atmosphere is witty and playful. Regulars set the mood and the special features 
of the place. 
Benefits and limitations 
According to Ray Oldenburg, third places provide many personal benefits. There is always some 
novelty; in the people choosing to attend this day, the topics of conversation, and the activities that 
will take place. Third Places contributes to a healthy perspective by combining pleasure with 
association with people in a wide group and affording the collective wisdom of its members 
(Oldenburg, 1989). They offer spiritual tonic and friends by the set. Finally, Third Places are good for 
housing friendships outside of the home. Third place friendships provide a sense of affiliation, a good 
complement to other more intimate relations – as humans, we need both a sense of intimacy and 
affiliation (Oldenburg, 1989).  
Third places serve the greater good too. As the conversation is the main activity, it ensures the 
exchange of ideas in the population and forms a good basis for a functioning democracy. The 
community life in third places promotes decency. People having a third place gain a feeling of 
ownership, which will - according to the architect and city planner Oscar Newman – make them look 
after the place and eventually protect it. Third places provide a space for relaxation and joy, which fits 
into the scheduled modern urban life and the compartmentalization of space according to activity or 
function (Oldenburg, 1989).  
Despite all the benefits Third Places may have for society, Ray Oldenburg also points out their limits. 
Third places shouldn’t be seen as the solution for all personal and social issues, and the type of 
association does not appeal to everyone.  Additionally, even when third places are accessible, they are 
not frequented by the majority of people. 
Virtual Third places 
Currently, third places are in decline, and a lot of communication has moved from being done 
physically to digitally. Massive Multiplayer Online Games (MMOs) are featuring extensive and persistent 
online 3D environments that are populated by hundreds of thousands of players at any given moment 
(Ducheneaut et al., 2007). These have been argued to be virtual third places, as they share similar 
characteristics as the third places described by Ray Oldenburg (Steinkuehler & Williams, 2006).  Both 
are inclusive social spaces with a “leveler” effect, bringing every new member on the same ground – 
status is earned through in-game achievements. MMOs also have their regulars. Ducheneaut argues 
that participation in MMOs is well suited for bridging social capital, not by providing deep emotional 
support but by exposing the individual to a diversity of worldviews. 
 
Towards a fourth place 
According to Arnault Morisson (2019), the distinction made by Ray Oldenburg between home (the 
first place), work (the second place) and the third place is dissolving in the knowledge economy. Co-
==PAGE:23==
 
23 
 
living combines the first and the second place, coworking the second and the third and comingling 
the first and the third place. 
Co-living spaces are for example spaces with shared apartments and an associated coworking space. 
Because of the increase in knowledge-based economy, the “digital nomad” lifestyle and mobile 
technologies, coworking spaces are expected to multiply worldwide (Yang et al., 2019). A comingling 
place is a e.g. a hotel featuring common areas and facilitating events. These new social environments 
– such FabLabs, Living Labs, Maker Spaces and shared living spaces – have been emerging during the 
last two decades because of new organizations of work, millennials’ preference to live in urban 
centers, the integration of work and personal life, the importance of informal networks and the 
importance of tacit knowledge (Morisson, 2019).  
Morisson describes the emergence of a new place, the Fourth Place, located in the intersection the 
first, second and third place (figure below). In fourth places, the barriers between work and leisure 
time, private and social dynamics are blurred. He encourages cities to favor the creation of fourth 
places by promoting mixed-use zoning, incorporating different dimensions of places.  
 
Figure 1:  Places before (left) and in (right) the knowledge economy (Morisson, 2019) 
 
Patricia Simões Aelbrecht defines fourth places as newly designed public areas and semi-public spaces 
where informal socialization occurs. These places are characterized by “in-betweenness”. They are not 
designed for a specific social usage – is it up to the users to decide how to use the space and whether 
to interact with others or not. Fourth places share similarities with third places: they are both inclusive, 
share similar social and behavioral characteristic, and are differentiated from home and work.  
However, as opposed to third places, conversation is not the main activity. Fourth places are about all 
activities conducted between necessary activities, such as ‘people-watching’, walking, waiting and 
‘killing time’ (Simões Aelbrecht, 2016). Another difference is the higher degree of “publicness”, giving 
them a more anonymous character. Aelbrecht argues that fourth places are more sociologically more 
open than third places and facilitates interaction between a diverse set of users. They are moving away 
from the old, homely and nostalgic atmosphere of third places, dictated by regulars. 
Ray Oldenburg provides a very good model for the “ideal” social place. It is interesting that being from 
1989, his description doesn’t include technological elements such as mobile phones, social media and 
digital advertisement. It is a snapshot of physical city entertainment, from a time without digital 
distractions. A lot of inspiration can be drawn from his extensive description of cafés, bars and other 
social spaces.  
==PAGE:24==
 
24 
 
 
However, the social settings have changed since 1989. As Morisson (2019) points it out, work and 
private life are getting more intertwined. Urban life and spaces are less compartmented, with the rise 
of co-working, co-living and co-mingling places (Morisson, 2019). Based on this we would argue that 
the role of third places and the dynamics inside should be adjusted to this new reality. 
 
In the next section, we will introduce the concept of social sustainability. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:25==
 
25 
 
3.1.3 Social sustainability 
 
While the economic and environmental aspects of sustainability are well defined and understood, the 
social component is being neglected (ScienceDirect, 2019). This aspect of sustainability, however, is 
crucial to understand when designing for the future of city entertainment. Circles of sustainability, a 
new approach usually applied for urban settlements (used among others by the United Nations) 
describes sustainability with four social domains. This brings the social aspect at the core of the 
concept of sustainability.  
Social sustainability is explained according to the two approaches: the widespread Triple bottom line 
(People-Profit-Planet), and the more recent Circles of Sustainability. We will then cover common 
applications and assessment methods of social sustainability. Finally, we will describe the role of 
communities in social sustainability.  
Social sustainability is the least understood and least defined dimension of sustainability, receiving 
significantly less attention than the economic and environmental concerns. It is defined in different 
ways, depending on the approach to sustainability 
 
Approach 1: Triple Bottom Line 
The most widespread approach to sustainability is a Venn Diagram, which describes it as the 
superposition of economic, environmental and social sustainability, here named respectively Profit, 
Planet and People. In this model, social sustainability refers to the latter, and encompasses among 
others : social equity, livability, health equity, community development, social capital, social support, 
human rights, labor rights, placemaking, social responsibility, social justice, cultural competence, 
community resilience, and human adaptation (Social Sustainability, n.d.). There are different schools 
regarding the dimensions of social sustainability. Nobel Laureate Amartya Sen proposes the following 
dimensions for social sustainability (Social Sustainability, n.d.): 
• 
Quality of life: the community ensures that basic needs are met and fosters a good quality of life 
for all members, in aera such as health, housing, education, employment and safety. 
• 
Equity: equitable opportunities and outcomes are provided for all of the communities’ members, 
especially for its most vulnerable members 
• 
Diversity: the community encourages and promotes diversity 
• 
Social Cohesion: the community provides processes, systems and structures that promote 
connectedness 
• 
Democracy & Governance: democratic processes and open and accountable governance 
structures are provided by the community 
 
Approach 2: Circles of Sustainability 
A more recent approach to sustainability, called Circles of Sustainability, divides sustainability into 4 
social domains: ecological, economic, political, and cultural sustainability. This method suggests that 
all the domains of sustainability are indeed social. This approach is developed through collaboration 
between organizations such as Metropolis, the UN Global Compact Cities Program, and is part of the 
larger Circles of Social Life project led by Paul James and Liam Magee (Urban Projectization, n.d.). The 
==PAGE:26==
 
26 
 
method stresses the importance of the social aspect of sustainability, stating that the particular words 
used to name each of the 4 domains are less important than the social space that the combinations of 
those words evoke (Circles of Sustainability, 2019). Paul James (2015) points out the limits of the Triple 
Bottom Line approach, treating economics as a domain outside of social life, and characterizing ecology 
as either an externality or a contextual resource (James, 2015). Designed as a practical tool for creating 
sustainable cities and communities (Circles of Sustainability, 2019), this method is usually applied for 
cities and urban settlements. Below is the profile circle for Sao Paulo, 2012, where the elements of the 
4 social domains are rated. 
 
Figure 2: Triple bottom line approach to sustainability (Pinterest, n.d.-b) 
 
Figure 3: An application of the approach Circles of Sustainabilty, here the Profile Circle of Sao Paulo, 2012 (SaintGeorgeIV, 
n.d.) 
==PAGE:27==
 
27 
 
Application and assessment 
Some dimensions of social sustainability are used as criteria for Socially Responsible Investing funds 
(SRI funds), such as community, diversity, employee relations, human rights and product safety. SRI 
funds are funds considering social/environmental good in their investments and seek to initiate 
positive social change. SIA (Social Impact Assessment) can be used by industrial projects to assess 
aspects of social sustainability. Examples of social impact assessments include looking at culture, 
community, political systems and how people live in a society (kinship or nuclear setting) (Vanclay, 
2003). Internationally recognized and accredited organizations have made certifications available to 
verify the social sustainability of products and services, such as The Forest Stewardship Council (paper 
and forest products), and the Kimberly Process (diamonds) (Social Sustainability, n.d.).  
The fact that social sustainability isn’t well-established could explain why social issues such as job 
satisfaction and social inequalities aren’t always efficiently tackled. We conclude that more 
development is needed to expand the field of knowledge of social sustainability. Experimenting with 
alternative social concepts, could lead to interesting findings (Beck, 2020). Proposing a concept with 
different social dynamics could help develop this neglected yet crucial aspect of sustainability. 
 
Community and sustainability 
In his book Urban Sustainability in Theory Practice (2015), Paul James links sustainability and 
community. We will explain how the perception of community has changed with urbanization, then 
introduce and describe the four current types of communities (as defined by Paul James). 
Understanding how different types of communities’ work is important if we want to strengthen social 
cohesion through city entertainment efficiently.  
The perception of community has changed with the impact of urbanization. Ferdinand Tönies (1963) 
talks about a shift from a society dominated by relatively stable rural communities, emphasizing 
mutual obligation and trust; to more mobile, highly urbanized societies with individual self-interest as 
a priority. He calls these states Gemeinschaften and Gesellscheften. However, according to James, this 
distinction is too dichotomous in the fluid, modern world. First, the exact definition of community is 
not that clear anymore. Community is commonly used to describe people from the same nation, 
ethnicity, religion or sexual orientation, and is often interchanged with Neighborhood (characterizing 
people living alongside others) in the West. He writes: community is no longer a relationship that a 
person might be drawn into, or even born into, without being forced at some time to think about its 
meaning, but for the most part we take such social relations for granted (James, 2015) 
He proceeds to actualize the definition of community as: 
“a group or network of persons who are connected (objectively) to each other by relatively durable 
social relations that extend beyond immediate genealogical ties and who mutually deﬁne that 
relationship (subjectively) as important to their social identity and social practice.” (James, 2015).  
He identifies 4 types of communities, based on the type of relation they embody: (1) grounded 
communities (people coming together in particular tangible localized settings based on face-to-face 
engagement), (2) cosmological communities (binding people together through a universalizing 
connection such as to God(s)), (3) lifestyle communities (where specific practices and attitudes are the 
key feature) and (4) projected communities (not bounded to a specific place or practice, where 
==PAGE:28==
 
28 
 
individuals engage in an open-ended processes of constructing, deconstructing and reconstructing 
identities and ethics for living) (James, 2015).  
While grounded communities tend to be more culturally rich than lifestyle communities the latter are 
seemingly more open and adaptive to change, by not being bound to a specific place. Grounded 
communities can also span over space, such as urban workers returning to their home village in the 
weekend. Cosmological communities tend to reinforce trust and mutual obligation between people 
following the specific moral codes of living. However, in the contemporary world, grounded 
communities tend to be drawn into cosmological communities which can cause friction. Since projected 
communities aren’t bound to a specific place, group of people or lifestyle, it is perhaps the most difficult 
to conceptualize. Projected communities can be seen as a means by which people create and re-create 
their lives with others (James, 2015). It is a very diverse and heterogenous group. On one side of the 
spectrum are “sustainable” communities such associations seeking politically expressed integration, 
communities of practice based on professional projects and associative communities which seek to 
enhance and support individual creativity, autonomy and mutuality (James, 2015). On the other side 
of the spectrum are trivial, manipulative or misleading communities, living off the modern search for 
meaning rather taking adequate action. James writes that these notions of ‘community’ might be 
projected by a corporate advertiser around a succession of engagements in the so-called third place of 
a Starbucks café or a self-named ‘creative city’ or ‘creative community’. Here older forms of community 
relations dissolve into postmodern ﬂuidity in which notions of settled, stable and abiding bonds 
between people recede into the background (James, 2015). 
The concept of projected community sounds especially appealing for us, as it is independent of space, 
type of people and type of activity. If our design intervention would encourage those type of 
community, these could theoretically foster diversity and inclusivity. Furthermore, their agility and 
adaptability make them well equipped to sustain themselves in the fast-paced modern world, 
especially in cities. If correctly developed, establishing and sustaining projected communities could be 
a good approach to reinstall a sense of community in urbanites life.  
We have now acquired a better understanding of social sustainability and recognized it’s limits. An 
experimental design solution could therefore be a good way to expand the knowledge in this field, that 
we define as crucial for making the world a better place. The works of Paul James (2015) gives us hope 
that a sense of community can be reinstalled in modern, fast-paced urban settings.  
The next part will cover the topic quality of life, something we wish to raise with our design 
intervention.  
 
3.1.4 Quality of Life 
 
One of our higher goals is to raise the general life quality of people, by offering a vison for the future 
of city entertainment. It is therefore important to understand how it is defined, measured and 
assessed. This will be covered in this section, as well as the discovered effect of physical activity, 
smartphone usage, music and dancing on physical and mental well-being.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:29==
 
29 
 
Definition 
Quality of life is defined by Britannica as the degree to which an individual is healthy, comfortable, and 
able to participate in or enjoy life events (Britannica, n.d.). Since quality of life refers both to one’s 
objective experience of life, and to ones living conditions, it is an ambiguous term. People value things 
differently – a disabled person may report a high quality of life, whereas a healthy person who recently 
lost a job may report a low quality of life (Britannica, n.d.). 
 
Measurement 
Being a partially subjective notion, quality of life is hard to measure. Recent work on the topic 
differentiate emotional wellbeing (the emotional quality of an individual’s everyday experience) and 
life evaluation (the thoughts people have about their life when they are asked about it) (Kahneman & 
Deaton, 2010). Emotional wellbeing is evaluated by asking people about the emotions they felt 
yesterday. Paraguay scored highest in a global 2019 report from Gallup (Gallup, 2019). Life evaluation 
is commonly evaluated with the Cantril scale, by asking people to rate their life on a scale from 0 to 
10, 0 being the worst possible life for one and 10 the best possible (Understanding How Gallup Uses 
the Cantril Scale, n.d.). 
 Studies have shown that income and education are more closely related to life evaluation, while 
health, care giving, loneliness, and smoking are relatively stronger related to emotional well-being Life 
evaluation and emotional well-being raises steadily with income. However, there is no further progress 
of emotional wellbeing beyond an annual income of ~$75,000. The study concludes that high income 
buys life satisfaction but not happiness, and that low income is associated both with low life evaluation 
and low emotional well-being (Kahneman & Deaton, 2010). 
One of the most commonly used international measure of development is the Human Development 
Index (HDI), combining life expectancy, education and per capita income indicators (Human 
Development Index, n.d.). The World Happiness Report is a landmark ranking 156 countries by their 
happiness levels (Wikipedia, n.d.-e); Finland was ranked number one in 2020. 
 
The effects of an active life on happiness 
Happiness is defined as “The state of pleasurable contentment of mind; deep pleasure in or 
contentment with one's circumstances.” (Dictionary, n.d.). In the scientific report “Happier People Live 
More Active Lives”, they were using smartphones to link the amount of happiness to physical activity. 
Not only did their findings reveal that individuals who are more physically active are happier, it also 
showed that individuals are happier in the moments when they are more physically active.  (Lathia et 
al., 2017) In other words, their research suggests that all forms of movement is related to happiness. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:30==
 
30 
 
Music engagement and wellbeing 
In a meta-ethnography that investigated 46 qualitative studies Perkins et al (2020) carefully selected 
and globally diverse, about how and in which ways participatory music engagement supports the 
mental well-being of people. In total, this included over 2100 participants between 2002 and 2019 
(Perkins, Mason-Bertrand, et al., 2020). 
The authors describe “complete mental health” as consisting of three components: emotional well-
being, psychological well-being, and social well-being. In their study, emotional well-being is referred 
to as the extent to which individuals experience happiness and evaluative well-being and life 
satisfaction. Psychological well-being is defined as the realization of human potential with six 
theoretically underpinned facets: purpose in life, self-acceptance, environmental mastery, positive 
relationships, autonomy, and personal growth. (Perkins, Mason-Bertrand, et al., 2020). 
Their findings demonstrate how music engagement supports the well-being of people in multiple ways. 
However, context and individual needs are essential in determining how positive these effects are. The 
effect of music participation is, among others, that it facilitates connections with other people, 
provides a means of emotional connection, provides a sense of purpose, supporting their self-
confidence, identity formation, and more. Furthermore, it connects people “with heritage, and with 
the past, providing opportunities to contribute to society, to feel togetherness and belonging, and to 
experience social support and enhanced social functioning” (Perkins, Mason-Bertrand, et al., 2020).  
From this meta-study, it becomes evident that music can be a powerful tool for self-expression, 
connection and happiness, and overall well-being in the right set and setting.  
Quality of life is a subjective notion, measured in different ways. Two aspects are distinguished: 
emotional wellbeing and life evaluation. It can be measured in different ways, such as with the Human 
Development Index and with subjective happiness scales. Physical activity, as well as participative 
music engagement has been linked with high levels of happiness.  
The next session will cover social connections. 
 
3.1.5 Social connection 
 
Understanding social connection is important when addressing the problem of disconnection. We 
want to make people feel more connected in the future of city entertainment. We are going to define 
what social connection is and show how its importance has increasingly been acknowledged. 
 
Definition 
Social connection is a feeling of belongingness to a group and a general feeling of closeness to other 
people. Brené Brown, author and Professor of Social Work at the University of Houston, defines 
connection as “the energy that exists between people when they feel seen, heard and valued; when 
they can give and receive without judgement; and when they derive sustenance and strength from the 
relationship” (Magazine, n.d.). Scientific evidence backs up that it is an essential human need.  
 
==PAGE:31==
 
31 
 
According to the motivational theory of Maslow's Hierarchy of Needs (1943), the social need, involving 
feelings of belongingness and social connection, is a fundamental basic human need. (McLeod, 2018). 
This includes friendships and family bonds with the biological family (parents, children, siblings) and 
with the chosen family (partners). There is on one side a need for emotional intimacy (ranging from 
sexual relationships to intimate emotional bonds), and on the other a need for membership in social 
groups (from belonging to a team of coworkers to forging an identity in a union or a club) 
(MasterClass, 2021). In his theory, the first and most essential human needs are physiological 
needs, among those air, food, and water and rest. Then comes the needs of security and safety, 
and only afterwards the need for love and belongingness. Moving up the hierarchy of needs comes 
the esteem need (including prestige and feeling of accomplishment), and finally the need for self-
actualization. This term refers to achieving one’s full potential, including creative activities. Later, 
Maslow himself criticized self-actualization being one’s ultimate need, and introduced the need for 
self-transcendence. By giving oneself to something beyond oneself (e.g., in altruism or spirituality), 
one would fully realize himself. 
 
Belonging 
However widespread in other fields than science, Maslow's Hierarchy of Needs (1943) is contested 
academically. According to Roy Baumeister (1995), the need for belongingness is as basic and primal 
as the need for water and food. In his famous experiment with monkeys, Harry Harlow changed how 
important we think attachment and love is in early stages of life. Infant monkeys were separated from 
their biological mothers and presented to two artificial ones. One was made of metal wire and provided 
food to the infant. The other was covered in soft rubber and terry cloth. The young monkeys would 
only go quickly to the wire mother to get food but spent the rest of the time with the cloth mother. 
When confronted to with a new situation, monkey infants with an artificial mother would explore the 
surrounding, run back to it and venture out again. This experiment showed that food isn’t the only 
primary need, that love, and affection are equally important in early life (Association for Psychological 
Science, n.d.). As mammals, humans are born fragile and helpless. We need strong emotional bonds 
not only for wellbeing, but also for survival. This could explain in an evolutionary perspective why 
humans seek and need connection, and why the lack of connection have negative consequences on 
our health (Lieberman, 2013).  
With his belongingness hypothesis, Roy Baumeister states that humans need frequent, nonaversive 
interactions within an ongoing relational bond, and that the need to belong is a powerful, fundamental, 
and extremely pervasive motivation (Baumeister & Leary, 1995). Humans naturally form social 
attachment under most conditions and resist the dissolution of their dissolution. Furthermore, “a lack 
of belongingness should constitute severe deprivation and cause a variety of ill effects.” (Baumeister & 
Leary, 1995). In a 2017 study including 1342 adolescents, Verhagen, Lauder and Baumeister also 
emphasizes that belongingness needs that exceed relationship satisfaction, regardless of the actual 
levels of both, contribute to actual health outcomes (Verhagen et al., 2018). A strong need for 
belonging is therefore nor detrimental per se. 
Different brain regions are processing social connection. The reward system of the brain is also 
involved in processing social connection and attention to loved ones (Social Connection - Wikipedia, 
n.d.). Studies suggests that social connection inhibits inflammatory gene expression and increase antiviral 
==PAGE:32==
 
32 
 
responses. (Cole et al., 2011). Likewise, performing acts of kindness has been found to have similar effects, 
suggesting that helping others provides similar health benefits (Social Connection - Wikipedia, n.d.). 
 
Social connections and wealth 
Our World in Data (2020) explores the link between social connections and wealth. In Europe, the most 
popular job searching strategy is through personal contacts and connections (Esteban & Roser, n.d.). 
Consequently, people with more and/or deeper social relations get better opportunies on the job 
market, overall leading to higher wealth. Our World in Data conclude by stating that social connections 
are not only important because they affect emotional well-being, but also because they affect material 
well-being too. Personal relations, even those that maintained for non-economic reasons, often give 
access to information and provide us with social collateral economic transactions – from buying a 
house to getting a job (Esteban & Roser, n.d.). 
We have now explained how social connections are perceived to be a fundamental need. Better social 
connections have even be linked to higher wealth (Esteban & Roser, n.d.).  Both intimate relations and 
a sense of belonging are needed to fulfill the need for social connections. This should be taken into 
consideration when designing for human connection. The next section explains the consequences 
when these social needs aren’t met. 
 
3.1.6 Loneliness 
 
A lack of social connections and belonging leads to loneliness, which is the unpleasant associated 
emotional response. Loneliness is a global health issue, according to studies it has a higher mortality 
rate than obesity (Cigna, 2018).  As we see city entertainment as an area suitable for – we see the 
potential to address loneliness in the future of the entertainment industry. Loneliness is a complex 
social phenomenon, that affects people differently according to many variables such as age and 
culture. A good understanding of the topic is needed to address loneliness efficiently. We will define 
loneliness and present how it is believed to affects health. We will discuss how loneliness is thought to 
evolve with age, and if there is a link between loneliness and use of technology. We will then explain 
how loneliness evolved over time, and what the current status. Finally, we will cover common ways of 
measuring it and addressing the issue. 
Cacioppo (2008) suggests that loneliness has evolved as a signal that one’s connections are wearing 
out or broken. It would be a motivation for people experiencing it to repair and maintain their 
connections to others, as these are needed for good health and well-being, as well as for the survival 
of one’s genes (Cacioppo & Patrick, 2008). Loneliness is commonly described as a subjective feeling, 
describing the gap between one’s social expectations and one’s actual social life (Jacobsen, 2019).  
 
 
 
==PAGE:33==
 
33 
 
Impact on health 
Individuals who lack social connections or report frequent feelings of loneliness tend to suffer higher 
rates of morbidity and mortality (Cornwell & Waite, 2009). The state of an individual’s social situation 
affects the expression of his genes. Studies suggest that loneliness can switch on/off genes regulating 
our immune system (Cole et al., 2011); and this would be what affects humans’ health (Ortiz-Ospina, 
2019a). 
Loneliness has actually been reported to have the same impact on mortality as smoking 15 cigarettes 
a day, making it even more dangerous than obesity (Cigna, 2018). However, according to Our World In 
Data, more studies are needed to prove the direct causality between loneliness and mortality, and to 
confirm the exact magnitude of its effects. Even studies examining the link between loneliness and 
mortality could suffer from omitted-variable bias (Ortiz-Ospina, 2019a) since genetic predisposition to 
loneliness may drive both loneliness and health outcomes (Abdellaoui et al., 2019).  
 
Loneliness and age 
Loneliness also touches younger people. According to a 2018 Cigna survey, Generation Z (people born 
between the mid-late 90’s and mid 2010’s) are the loneliest, and students have higher loneliness scores 
than retirees. In rich countries, younger people are more likely to report feeling lonely (Ortiz-Ospina, 
2019b). Loneliness among the youth is an important issue, because they are at the age in which a lot 
of one’s identity is constructed and there is a higher need of feeling validated, reassured, heard, valued 
(The Mary Foundation, 2020). Longitudinal studies have shown that loneliness levels vary through 
throughout one’s life, generally following a “U-curve”; people are more lonely in their youth, then 
loneliness diminishes during adulthood and grows again late in life (Pinquart & Sörensen, 2001). Two 
factors could explain this development. A direct factor is that as the individual grows, his social 
expectation lowers and he becomes more selective in his social relations, surrounding himself with 
people bringing positive emotions. An indirect factor is the loss of relative and friends coupled with 
the deterioration of health. The direct factor dominates in the middle ages, while the indirect takes 
over in the late ages, which could explain the “U-curve”  (Ortiz-Ospina, 2019b). This could explain why 
the generation Z is the loneliest generation.  
Loneliness also touches younger people. According to a 2018 Cigna survey, Generation Z (people born 
between the mid-late 90’s and mid 2010’s) are the loneliest, and students have higher loneliness scores 
than retirees. In rich countries, younger people are more likely to report feeling lonely (Ortiz-Ospina, 
2019b). Loneliness among the youth is an important issue, because they are at the age in which a lot 
of one’s identity is constructed and there is a higher need of feeling validated, reassured, heard, valued 
(The Mary Foundation, 2020). Longitudinal studies have shown that loneliness levels vary through 
throughout one’s life, generally following a “U-curve”; people are more lonely in their youth, then 
loneliness diminishes during adulthood and grows again late in life (Pinquart & Sörensen, 2001). Two 
factors could explain this development. A direct factor is that as the individual grows, his social 
expectation lowers and he becomes more selective in his social relations, surrounding himself with 
people bringing positive emotions. An indirect factor is the loss of relative and friends coupled with 
the deterioration of health. The direct factor dominates in the middle ages, while the indirect takes 
==PAGE:34==
 
34 
 
over in the late ages, which could explain the “U-curve”  (Ortiz-Ospina, 2019b). This could explain why 
the generation Z is the loneliest generation.  
 
Link with technology 
Understanding how technology affects loneliness is important for us. If we choose to implement 
technology in our design intervention, negative social consequences should be prevented. A 2018 
global study conducted by the Kaiser Foundation analyses the role technology plays in loneliness. 
Across countries, more people say technology has made it harder to spend time with friends and family 
in person than say it has made it easier. However, social media is seen as a way to strengthen one’s 
social connection. But those experiencing loneliness in the UK and US are divided on whether social 
media helps or makes it worse (DiJulio et al., 2018). Cigna (2018) could not observe any correlation 
between social media use and loneliness.  However, Cigna highlights that people frequently engaging 
in meaningful in-person interactions have much lower loneliness scores than those who rarely interact 
with others face-to-face (Cigna, 2018). These two findings seem contradictory, and more investigation 
may be needed explain this further explain this phenomenon 
 
Evolution of loneliness 
The term "loneliness” first appeared in English in the 18th century (The New Yorker, 2020). In her book 
A Biography of Loneliness (2019), historian Alberti argues that chronic loneliness did not really didn’t 
exist before the nineteenth century (Alberti, 2019). She argues that since it was impossible to survive 
without living among other people, loneliness didn’t last long. She believes that loneliness appeared 
with capitalism and secularism. She accuses the individualistic philosophy to drive many of the 
separations and hierarchies that have developed since the 18th century (self/world, 
individual/community, public/private). She points out that an unprecedent amount of people live 
alone, which is causing be causing more loneliness.  
While this argumentation seems intuitive, current data does not support these claims. Reported 
loneliness is not higher in so called “individualistic” societies (e.g. Norway) and lower in so-called 
“collectivistic” societies (e.g., Mexico) (Hofstede Insights, n.d.). In fact, countries with the lowest 
reported loneliness are considered very individualistic, such as Denmark and Switzerland (Our World 
in Data, 2019). Living alone has not been associated with higher levels of loneliness either (Ortiz-
Ospina, 2019b). In fact, low levels of loneliness have been reported in countries where many people 
live on their own. It should be noted that people tend to live more on their own in richer countries 
(Share of One-Person Households vs. GDP per Capita, 2016, n.d.). 
 
Current situation 
Many headlines suggests that loneliness is on the rise, calling out a “loneliness epidemic” (Ortiz-Ospina, 
2019b). However, Our World in Data (2019) warns against using the popular term of “loneliness 
epidemic”. In facts, studies in the US and in Sweden have shown that loneliness level among older 
people haven’t risen in the last decade (Is the “epidemic of Loneliness” Fake News? - UnHerd, n.d.), 
==PAGE:35==
 
35 
 
(Ortiz-Ospina, 2019b). The same seem to apply for adolescents; a studies conducted on American high 
school students since 1977 revealed a slight decrease in loneliness for the same age group over that 
period of time (Ortiz-Ospina, 2019b). This doesn’t contradict the fact the generation Z is the most 
lonely, as loneliness may individually decrease as one grows (Pinquart & Sörensen, 2001).   
 
Measurement 
Loneliness is measured by asking people questions, so it is almost always self-assessed. It can be 
measured directly (by asking “How often do you feel lonely?”) or indirectly, by asking bout emotions 
associated with loneliness, from which loneliness is then inferred (UK Office for National Statistics, 
n.d.). For example, the UCLA loneliness scale consists of 20 statements, such as “There are people who 
really understand me”, to which surveyed have to respond Never, Rarely, Sometimes or Often. It should 
also be noted that there is variation in how people understand the term “loneliness” and that some 
people might be reluctant to admit that they are feeling lonely. This might be particularly true for 
certain groups such as older men (UK Office for National Statistics, n.d.). Could it be possible that some 
people, missing a good reference point for social connections, would downplay their loneliness 
(because they don’t know better)? If this is the case, then self-assessment may not be an accurate way 
of measuring loneliness. 
 
Measures taken 
The reduction of loneliness has long been a motive for human activity and social organization 
(Wikipedia, n.d.-c). A common way of treating loneliness is therapy, but not all patients can afford this 
solution. Doctors can refer these to NGO’s and community-led projects, fighting loneliness across the 
globe. Measures vary from scheduling meetings with two people, from facilitating large group 
activities. Certain governments step in to fight loneliness. In Netherlands, there is a phone line for older 
adults feeling lonely – England has in 2018 become the first country with a ministry of loneliness. Pet 
therapy has also been used as an attempt to treat loneliness and depression. Technology companies 
have been advertising their products as helpful for reducing loneliness. In 1905 early telephones were 
presented as a way for isolated farmers to reduce loneliness (Wikipedia, n.d.-c). Mindfulness apps and 
apps helping strangers connect by arranging meetings are example of technological solutions tackling 
the issue. AI is also used – as a virtual companion or in a physical manifestation, such as the social 
robot Pepper (SoftBank Robotics, n.d.). Looking at the limited efficacity of these solutions, we believe 
that there is a need for improvement.   
Science suggests that loneliness is a mechanism to signal that one’s social connections are wearing of, 
coming from times where social isolation would lead to death. Loneliness is currently a major health 
issue (supposedly deadlier than obesity (Cigna, 2018)) and is believed to switch on/off genes regulating 
our immune system (Cole et al., 2011). Loneliness is believed to follow a “U-curve” through life, a 
person feeling loneliest in the beginning and at the end of life. More studies are needed to describe 
the link between loneliness and use of technology. Even if loneliness levels are high and has huge 
negative effects on the global health and wellbeing, a global increase in loneliness hasn’t been 
observed. 
==PAGE:36==
 
36 
 
We have now covered the following topics: Third Places, social sustainability, quality of life, social 
connections, and loneliness. Parallel to our broad research, we conducted interviews with experts, to 
study our domain from a different angle. The rest of this chapter will describe our interview process. 
3.2 Focused Interviews 
Moving on from the broad research stage, we continued with a focused approach consisting of in-
depth interviews and further reading from our gathered resources. Knowing we wanted to work with 
events, we were having a “top down” approach, reaching out to a broad spectrum of organizers and 
relevant stakeholders with the aim of learning from the best.  
The way we (all 3 writers of this paper) were fortunate enough to come across some of the people 
interviewed, were through participating in the European Leadership Summit (ELS) 2019 in Aarhus. It 
was a knowledge-sharing summit between organizers of 30+ regional Burning Man events across 
Europe. It was easy to reach out to them and discuss what they saw as the future of the event industry. 
In parallel, research about existing proclaimed sustainable and alternative events and venues was 
conducted. 
The idea with the 12 interviews was to get as much insight as possible on all aspects of event 
organization and facilitation, learning, among other things, what makes an event truly good and 
inclusive for its participants.  
We felt from the beginning that entertainment could be far more meaningful that what is proposed 
right now. In 10 years, cities should offer quality in this sector, going beyond simply “being 
entertained”. We therefore focused on so-called “transformative events” 
• 
What makes an event transformative? 
• 
In what way are you involved in transformative events? 
• 
Have you felt the effect of transformative events yourself? 
The interviews were later used in the project as they took part of the generation of context factors. 
They were also useful to get in insight on the domain we chose to work with in the project.

==PAGE:38==
