Chapter 4:  
Reframing city entertainment  
 
 
 
==PAGE:39==
 
39 
 
This chapter follows the steps of the Vision in Product Design (ViP) process. You will be led through the 
context-, interaction- and product level. Each step from 1 (establishing the domain/time) to 7 
(concepting and detailing) will be detailed here, while step 8 (designing and detailing) will have a 
chapter on its own. The chapter starts with a summary of the Deconstruction of the current context, in 
the domain of entertainment with socializing purposes. 
Preparation: Deconstruction  
In this part we describe the procedures we carried out as a preparation for the project. The key findings 
will be presented here, while the 3 individual deconstruction exercises can be read in Appendix A. 
Our product/objects of analysis were 3 different gathering places. Viewing each of them as a closed 
system, we analyzed and deconstructed them on the three levels of ViP - product, interaction, context 
level. Some examples of questions we had in mind while deconstructing: 
• 
How would the user interact with these artifacts?  
• 
How do they look like?  
• 
Why do they look that way?  
• 
What kind of mindset were created with in the past?  
We carried out the exercise by analyzing: 
1. A regular 16+ club in Copenhagen, Denmark (nightclub for people above 16) 
2. Masada, a cultural association in Milan, Italy 
3. A cathedral in Stavanger, Norway  
We chose to analyze very different places to get an overview of the diversity of our domain. Even if a 
church isn’t commonly defined as entertainment, it is still a third place where people meet, and we 
found it interesting to analyze the interior and the social dynamics inside. 
From this analysis, we discovered that the 16+ club mostly attracts underaged people to get easy 
access to intoxication and lose themselves in a so-called social way. However, we discovered that at 
these places people seem quite disconnected from each other. Almost everyone seems to be engaged 
in different activities, while not even being part of their group. On the other hand, the two other places 
performed higher on the togetherness scale, albeit in a different way.  
In Masada, people have a perceived feeling of connection to each other, and they are genuinely nice 
and eradiate a feeling of connectedness – which can be due to the membership structure of the club. 
Every participant must apply for membership for the club, meaning the ones who are not interested 
enough are not going to make it.  
In Stavanger Cathedral the people attend together, but individually, solemn ritualized events, focusing 
their attention outwards while listening to the priest. Contrary to the cathedral, clubs like Masada 
bring attention more to the self, with active participation in the crowd, where metaphorically, one’s 
ego gets dissolved through the ritual of dancing. 
 
==PAGE:40==
 
40 
 
By looking at their environments, we can more easily understand the dynamics that influence the 
interactions and the atmospheres of these three places. The 16+ club is described as a place which is 
trying to be sophisticated in its in their elements, but even while the disharmony of said elements 
breaks down the atmosphere and communicates a cheap and commercial feeling. Most likely the 
designer wanted to create a glamorous eye-catching environment where the teenagers can feel like 
more adults, so the room is full of clichéd objects – chandeliers - meant to resemble glamourous 
surroundings. This designer did not focus on how to make the environment good and safe for the 
young age group.  
In contrast, the Milanese club’s environment does not strive to be fancy and posh looking, and it 
attracts a very diverse - sometimes also famous/important - but equally open-minded and friendly 
group of people. The living room-looking environment gives a cozy atmosphere to the place. People 
are more at ease here and engage in random discussions with strangers.  
Meanwhile, Stavanger Church is closer to Masada in how mindfully it is designed to fit for the purpose. 
It has impressive decorations and ornaments to highlight the place’s importance upon the 
churchgoers. Yet the design of the environment has only a single purpose. It is meant to guide the 
attention of any viewer towards the elevated altar where the performance takes place. This is a 
hierarchical structure between us and them (attendances and priests). Contrary to this, the DJ platform 
is on the same ground level as the dancefloor in the case of Masada. This gives a feeling of less 
hierarchical structure on the dance floor which has a positive effect on the atmosphere and removes 
the barrier between us and them (participants and DJ). 
To summarize the deconstruction, we can say that the 16+ club is focusing on helping youngsters get 
cheap entertainment while they are "losing themselves”. This club has a very specific target group and 
is mostly not appealing for anyone above that certain age. While we acknowledge the needs and 
benefits of this type of entertainment, it is not this direction we will follow with our vision. The genuine 
interactions observed at Masada appeal more to us.  Masada is drastically different; it can be described 
as a city in the city where once you enter you have lost a sense of time and space because of the 
mindfully designed environment and the togetherness of the people. While Stavanger Cathedral seems 
like a more cold and rigid environment for rituals and solemn togetherness where people are 
somewhat engaged in certain activities, but their attention is mostly concentrated upon the preaching 
of the priest and it is not interactive nor participatory. 
 
Analyzing three drastically different social settings (a 16+ club, a cultural institution and a church) gave 
us a good overview of the diversity in the fields of physical entertainment. Working on three layers of 
description prepared us mentally for the Designing part. This process guides the designer through same 
three levels - but instead of looking at existing solutions, we look at for possibilities laying in the future. 
Having now looked at the current situation of physical entertainment, we will proceed to the designing 
part, looking at the future instead of the present. 
 
==PAGE:41==
 
41 
 
Designing: 
This is the main part of the design process, describing our steps and presenting our design 
intervention at a conceptual level.   
Part 1: Future context 
The Future context chapter is the foundation stone of the project. It includes the first four steps of 
the ViP process. It starts with establishing the domain and goes until the vision statement which will 
be the first building block of the desired future. 
1. Establishing the domain/time: 
 
The domain is used as a lens to observe a social phenomenon. The domain should not be too narrow 
as it will limit the types of interventions. Since we were not collaborating with a specific company, no 
constraints were imposed upon us, nor were we advised in any specific solution area.  
We decided to work with the domain “Entertainment with socializing purposes in the city”. 
In addition, we specified the timeframe of our design intervention to be 10 years in the future from 
now: year 2031. It was far enough in the future to allow us to design something different from the 
current reality. Given the time of the project (4,5 month), 10 years is ambitious, as there were many 
factors and much uncertainty to take into consideration. 
Our intention behind the domain: 
• 
Entertainment: “an event, performance, activity or action, providing amusement or enjoyment 
for one.” (Entertainment Definition, n.d.) 
• 
Socialize: “to associate or mingle sociably with others” (Definition of Socialize, n.d.) 
• 
Purpose: “an intended or desired result; end; aim; goal”(Definition of Purpose, n.d.) 
 
2. Generation of context factors:  
 
In this chapter we are introducing the context factors we made from our research. 
ViP is a context-driven approach, and the context factors are the building blocks of the future context 
we are designing. They can be observations, thoughts, laws, considerations, which are relevant and 
have a certain degree of novelty - they can emerge from various places for example: literature, 
interviews, personal observation, laws, general beliefs etc. For example, developing trends and socio-
cultural principles that have an influence on our domain. There are four types of factors: trends, 
development, states, and principles; here explained with examples from this project. 
 
 
==PAGE:42==
 
42 
 
 
Trends refer to shared experiences, beliefs and behaviors that are in flux (Tromp & Hekkert, 2018).  
• 
Example: There might be less resources allocated to city nightlife after COVID-19 
Developments are also in flux, but on a larger level and usually over a larger time scale. They can for 
example describe biological, political, demographic, or technological developments.  
• 
Example: People are moving to the cities. 
States are static descriptions of the current context, where no change is expected in the near future. 
• 
Example: Copenhageners are big CO2-emitters. 
Principles are fundamental laws governing human life. They can be mathematical principles, laws of 
physics, universal principles of human nature, affecting the past, present and future context. 
• 
Example: Rejection hurts the same way as physical pain 
 
The context factors were generated from the extensive research documented in the “Appendix B.1 – 
Generation of context factors”. We also used experts’ interviews (see Chapter from the chapter 3.3 
Focused interviews to increase the variety and diversity of the context factors. Together we were able 
to have a profound understanding of the domain from many different aspects. In the end we had an 
overwhelming amount of 469 context factors in total. 
 
 
Figure 4: Visualisation of the categories of our context factors. The size of each heading is proportionnal to 
the amount of context factors it contains 
==PAGE:43==
 
43 
 
Mapping out the context factors 
To start analyzing this data first we divided them into categories. This helped us understand and 
visualize the distribution and quantity of context factors between the different categories. From 469 
factors, these turned into 23 distinct categories, visualized above, where the bigger the text, the more 
context factors it contained. (top figure on page).  
The following is the list of the top five categories which had the most factors in them:  
1. Human connection (42) 
2. Transformation (39) 
3. Human behavior (37) 
4. Society (28) 
5. Events (28) 
Key takings from the generation of context factors: 
 
This is a non-exhaustive summary of the most important thoughts we derived from categorizing of the 
factors. We cherry picked the most surprising and the most motivating ones, as the ViP method 
encourages: 
The word “entertainment” is very limiting: 
 
Organizers of so-called “transformative events” don’t define their events as “entertainment”. What 
they facilitate goes beyond entertaining. When someone is being entertained, he is taking a break from 
the world, from himself. A transformative event, to the contrast, requires personal physical and mental 
involvement and the willingness to put effort into it. It is not about losing oneself but finding oneself. 
We want to raise the standard of the entertainment industry, and go beyond its primal function, that 
of simply entertaining the population. 
 
A few basic needs are fueling most of the technological development: 
 
Although amazing and diverse our technological innovation may seem, the vast majority are just means 
to fulfill 5 primal goals dictated by our striatum (our “reptilian brain”) for our survival as a species: 
getting food, getting sexual partners, getting information about our environment, using as little energy 
as possible and getting social status (Bohler, 2019). Parallelly, we also all have a need for communing 
and for making sense of the world (Cheissoux, n.d.). 
 
A Recipe for “transformative events”: 
 
Collected by interviewing multiple organizers of so-called “transformative events”, beautifully 
summed up by Harley Dubois (co-founder of Burning Man), here are the 4 building blocks to make an 
event transformative: 
 
 
==PAGE:44==
 
44 
 
1. Provide a safe environment (physically and mentally) 
2. Take people out of their comfort zone  
3. Make the people work together and experience together 
4. Ritualize the event like a ceremony 
 
We generated a large quantity (469) of context factors, and we categorized them into 23 categories. 
By categorizing them into groups we saw some underlying structure and trends that we found 
inspiring. In the next chapter we are going to cluster the context factors and use them to build a future 
context 
3. Structuring the context 
 
In this step we are reducing the number of context factors, clustering them into a meaningful order. In 
doing so an underlying structure of the future world might arise, which will create the foundation for 
our main statement.  
Clustering of Context factors 
Clustering the Context factors (not the same as categorizing presented in the previous section) is one 
of the most arduous steps of the design process. The seemingly chaotic information should be put into 
a meaningful order. In this step, the variety and complexity of the context factors is reduced to develop 
a clear and consistent picture of the future world.  
First, we looked for interesting and relevant factors to work with. To dig deeper into the meaning 
behind these statements, we systematically and creatively combined context factors, creating clusters. 
There are two types of clusters:  
• 
A common quality cluster is the combination of two or more context factors based on their 
same underlying direction.  
o Example: Slow and long-sighted developments are not exciting (principle) + Society is 
built so that loans are inevitable (state) + Usually people do not think extremely far in 
the future: “who will be the next president?” (state) = People are not supported in 
long-term planning. 
 
• 
An emergent quality cluster combines context factors by bringing different factors together, 
making a cluster emerges from their underlying information.  
o Example: Weather/climate influence people's mood/culture (state) + Emotional 
response is faster than cognitive (principle) + Dopamine drives motivated behavior, not 
actual happiness (principle) = We are unable to control the forces of nature and our 
own biology. 
We did this process in multiple iterations to reduce the vast amount of data we gathered. The ViP 
method argues that factors which does not have novelty to them should not be taken into 
consideration. Therefore, factors which did not state relevant or importance in understanding the 
domain were removed. The next pages present 8 clusters, each presenting an aspect of our future 
context.  
 
==PAGE:45==
 
45 
 
 
Image 3: Saturnalia- Ancient roman festival in the honor of Saturn. Roman social norms were reversed this day – slaves were 
treated like their masters and were allowed to disrespect them for a day. Gambling (normally prohibited) was authorized and  
married couples could have affairs (School Of Life, 2016) (Saturnalia - Wikipedia, n.d.) 
The Forgotten Importance of Rituals 
Religion used to facilitate such rituals, where people gathered for an occasion, often of an underlying 
spiritual or psychological nature. People could reconnect to greater ideas, be purged, or mark a 
transformation helping to grow. As the world entered industrialization, priorities switched from the 
spiritual wellbeing of society to economic growth, resulting in a lot of rituals and celebrations being 
cut out of the calendar (School Of Life, 2016). The function of rituals could be revived through a new 
type of entertainment in the future. 
Context factors:

emotional, the personal and 
the moral can be secular 
(Principle)

important dimension of social 
life, yet are not currently 
common (State) 
 
 
Nature has a calming effect on 
people (Principle) 
 
As people are becoming more 
mindful, they realize more the 
consequence of their actions 
(Principle)

self-development (Principle) 
 
Big gathering could have 
spread agricultural knowledge 
between isolated groups of 
hunter-gatherers (State) 
 
As people are expanding their 
horizon, they seem to 
rediscover spirituality (Trend) 
 
People are becoming more 
spiritual (Trend)

Simultaneously Looking for a Tribe and Trying to Be Unique 
Those two apparently opposite needs coexist within everyone. It is in our human nature to find peers 
who share values and interests (Greene, 2018). Likewise, we believe that everyone has a creative soul 
that needs the right medium and environment to be expressed. In a healthy community, the individual 
should therefore be encouraged to be himself.

of being a healthy and happy 
human (Principle)

(Principle)

Image 5: (Daily Mail Online, n.d.) Loneliness has been reported to be as deadly as smoking 15 cigarettes a day (Cigna, 2018)  
Loneliness and Anxiety 
Globally an estimated 284 million people experienced an anxiety disorder in 2017, making it the most 
prevalent mental health or neurodevelopmental disorder (Ritchie & Roser, 2020). Lowered stigma 
around it, underreporting in the past as well as social media and poor sleep habits could explain this 
rise. We live in the most connected times in history, yet self-reported loneliness is very high. Loneliness 
does not only touch elder generations - In the UK, 60% of 18 to 34 years old say they often feel lonely 
(Kurzgesagt - In a Nutshell, 2019). Loneliness is not isolation, it is a feeling happening when there is a 
gap between one’s social expectation and the reality (Jacobsen, 2019). Extraversion and a large friend 
group does not protect from loneliness. Loneliness works like a negative feedback-loop, where social 
interactions are perceived more negatively than they really are, leading to more loneliness. Because 
of this negative feedback loop, it seems hard to tackle loneliness by creating a new regular venue.  
 
Context factors: 
 
Approximately six in ten 
Americans feel that their 
interests and ideas are not 
shared by those around them 
(trend) 
 
56% of Americans feels 
(sometimes or always) like the 
people around them are not 
necessarily with them (trend) 
 
The feeling of isolation, of 
difference from the group, is 
depressing and terrifying 
(principle) 
 
People are afraid of winding up 
alone (principle) 
 
More than half of Americans 
often feel like no one knows 
them well (trend) 
 
For integration to happen at 
all, people have to meet each 
other (principle) 
==PAGE:48==
 
48 
 
 
Image 6: (Eye Center Texas, n.d.) 
Online and Offline Life Interference 
Smartphones and social media platforms have been adopted extremely quickly, and the creation of an 
online identity has followed. The algorithms behind social media platforms are used as money 
machines. Data is collected and used to build models predicting its users’ actions. Manipulation 
techniques and psychology are used to keep the user’s attention, so they are more exposed to 
advertisement (Orlowski, 2020). Because of this, a lot of people’s attention has deviated from the 
offline world to the online one. Social media users tend to present themselves as more desirable than 
they are in real life, creating the impression that everyone is better off, which tends to lower one’s 
self-esteem. Also, social media users engage in actions in the offline world to boost their identity in 
the online world. We would argue that smartphones use should be discouraged in venues. Boosting 
one’s online persona should not be the motive to attend events. 
Context factors: 
 
People behave differently 
online than in real life (Trend) 
 
Our attention span is getting 
shorter and shorter 
(Development) 
 
Our smartphones continuously 
interrupt our attention (State) 
Social media is causing anxiety 
(State) 
 
People tend to present 
themselves as more interesting 
online (Principle) 
Social life is shifting from the 
physical to the online space 
while making a negative 
impact on our empathic skills 
(Trend) 
 
Things seem more perfect 
from the outside than what 
they are in reality (Principle) 
 
Social media is a new platform 
of self-expression and plays a 
big role in our everyday life 
(Development) 
 
As digital technologies 
advanced, they decreased the 
likeliness of random sincere 
human connections 
(Development) 
 
We are overusing social media 
which creates unrealistic 
expectations (Trend) 
 
People come to serve a 
function—not to bond with, 
but to feed your insecurities 
(State) 
 
==PAGE:49==

Image 7: The participatory non-profit festival Borderland (Denmark) serve as a gathering place for artists,  
who make art and experiences for each other (Esser, n.d.). 
The New Age of Experimental Underground Events 
 
There has always been an underground scene parallel to the mainstream scene. Successful 
underground artists and venues become popular and end up in the mainstream culture. Underground 
alternative events are usually more authentic, especially when profit is left out of the equation. In the 
search for more meaningful and creative event-making, we may see a rise in experimental 
underground events. For example, at Burning Man, people come before money. This has made it 
possible to grow increasingly inclusive, “radical inclusiveness” being one of their 10 core principles. In 
profit venues, guests can feel like products, encouraged to buy alcohol for example. By valuing what 
guests have to offer instead of how much they can spend, they feel more invested and connected to 
the place or event. 
Context factors: 
 
Compared to regular consumer 
events, participation-based 
events are being co-created 
with the audience (Principle) 
 
With our smart phones a 
crowd is just individuals rather 
than a crowd (Principle) 
 
People are limited because 
they just try to make money 
(Trend) 
 
Big entertainment industries 
are becoming soulless 
compared to small intimate 
gatherings (Trend) 
 
There are more festive events 
without alcohol (Development) 
 
In capitalism, doing something 
for the love of it has less 
intrinsic value than doing 
something fulfilling a need 
(Principle) 
 
After COVID-19, the 
underground scene and house 
parties might take over clubs 
(Development) 
 
There might be less resources 
allocated to partying after 
COVID-19 (Trend) 
 
In capitalism, doing something 
for the love of it has less 
intrinsic value than doing 
something fulfilling a need 
(Principle) 
==PAGE:50==

Image 8: (Pinterest, n.d.-a) 
 
 
Everyone Can Become an Artist 
We believe that everyone has had at some point creative inspiration. However, societal norms have 
not made it easy for artistic souls to express themselves fully. Art as an occupation has the reputation 
for being economically unsustainable. Furthermore, with a global average of 40-44 working hours per 
week (Wikipedia, n.d.-d), family life and other responsibilities, there seem to be little time left for 
artistic pursuits. However, with the raising of living standards, and changing of mentally, there might 
be more time and more acceptance for doing art. We want to channel this emerging need for creative 
self-expression in the future of entertainment. 
Context factors: 
 
There are social norms 
dictating how to be happy 
(State) 
 
People like convenience and 
comfort (State) 
 
Stigma can make it difficult for 
people to express who they 
really are (Principle) 
 
Art resonates differently in 
everyone (Principle) 
Humans instinctively compare 
themselves to the other 
humans (Principle) 
 
Everyone likes to feel special 
(Principle) 
 
Many multi-talented people 
are out there with a bunch of 
weird interests (State) 
 
In art there is a social economy 
- money is not the important 
thing, it’s how good people are 
at their skill that matters. 
(Principle) 
 
People are under pressure with 
their job and Corona (Trend) 
 
People prefer things to happen 
naturally (Principle) 
 
 
==PAGE:51==
 
51 
 
 
Image 9: (Havas Media, n.d.) 
A Need for Meaningfulness 
The way we are managing and consuming finite resources in a seemingly infinite way is senseless and 
highly unsustainable. In the modern world, people tend to feel powerless towards their leaders’ 
decisions, and towards the way earths’ resources are overexploited. A high percentage of people are 
dissatisfied with their job (19,4% in Europe (“Quality of Life in Europe,” 2016) and more than 50% in 
the US (Kelly, 2019)). Europeans are pessimistic about the future (53% of brits and 73% of French 
believe that the future generation will have a lower standard of living (Guardian, 2014)). Happiness 
levels have not grown as people are getting more material possessions. Instead, it quickly returns to a 
relatively stable level. It seems like we are unable to enjoy the results of this overproduction. We call 
for less materialistic and more meaningful forms of entertainment in the future.

The Entertainment Industry Reflects the Desires of Society 
Gatherings and celebrations are reflections of society. Society affects the entertainment industry, 
which in return affects how people behave, which has repercussions on society. Social gatherings can 
lead to integration and transformation, but also to disconnection, intoxication, and extreme 
consumerism. Social gatherings are part of the foundation of society and should be taken very 
seriously.

Clustering context factors helped giving an overview over all information contained in the  individual 
context factors. The result is 8 aspects of the present and future of the physical entertainment industry. 
Later, we will uncover the relationships between the clusters we identified. In the next part, we will 
present you a picture of our future context: city entertainment in 2031.  
==PAGE:53==
 
53 
 
The Future: 
We will now describe the main patterns in our 
future context – city entertainment in 2031. Two 
major trends will be introduced to you. Then, the 
structure of our future context will be presented. 
Based on our research and interviews with experts, 
we have crafted two scenarios for the future of city 
entertainment: A Likely Future and a Desired 
Future. After introducing these scenarios, we will 
discuss how technology could impact the future of 
city entertainment. 
Megatrends 
We have identified 2 major developments, or Megatrends (CIFS, 2009) that we believe will have a 
global impact in 2031. Megatrends are seemingly inevitable – it is not a question if they will happen 
but when they will happen (Beck, 2020).  Before we continue, we will shortly introduce them to you, 
to set the scene and transport you to the next decade: 
Megatrend 1: Climate change is now 
Based on the current the current failure to adequately tackle climate change, it is almost certain that 
its impact will be felt on a global scale. More intense weather, more frequent and bigger wildfires (Heat 
Waves and Climate Change, n.d.), floods threatening coastal cities will lead to large population 
movements of climate refugees (sea level rise alone could displace more than 100 million people 
(Climate Impacts, n.d.)), causing more political instability. Climate change is likely to be more 
considered at governmental, business, and individual level. Because of this, contributing in some way 
to another to the fighting of climate change may be necessary for our concept to be relevant in 2031. 
Megatrend 2: The rise of AI 
We predict great technological advancement in all areas, empowering the individual. The rise of AI will 
lead to a productivity boom (Lichfield, 2020), and its predictions will give technology god-like qualities 
(Harari, 2016). The social implications are huge and are very different, depending on how humanity 
decides to use this technology. For example AI can analyze large quantities of health data and discover 
patterns, leading to new discoveries in medicine improvement of individual diagnostics (European 
Parliament, n.d.). However, AI can also be used for autonomous weapons, permitting armed conflict 
of greater scale, fought at timescales faster than humans can comprehend (University of Ottowa, 
2017). 
These two trends did not emerge from the generation of context factors, but from research about 
global predictions for the next decade. This should be taken into consideration later when designing 
the intervention.  
The next step is to make sense of the identified clusters of context factors, and build a future 
context. 
 
==PAGE:54==
 
54 
 
Future context 
 
The clusters of context factors were then used to form a coherent view of the future. In this process, 
we realized that many clusters were pointing at conflicting forces, so we placed them in a 2-axis polar 
system, according to the recommendations of the ViP-method. 
The following matrixes represents the structure of our future context. Each axis points towards two 
different futures, describing how people behave differently in each of them. It is worth mentioning a 
person is nuanced and can be several of these – yet often choose to play on one side of the axis. The 
first places our clusters within our context, and the second is a more simplified version. 
One axis describes the general behavior of an urbanite who wishes to socialize. On one end is the 
“consumer” mode, passively experiencing what is exposed to. On the other end is the “creator” mode, 
challenging the status quo and proposing something different. The other axis describes the urbanite’s 
attitude toward society. On one end of the spectrum is the highly individualistic behavior, where 
personal values are cultivated, and personal goals are prioritized over common ones. On the other side 
is the “collectivistic behavior”, where common values and goals take the first priority.

The matrix helped us structure our clusters in a coherent way and will form the basis for our two 
future scenarios: the likely future and the desired future. We will now introduce you to each of them.

Likely Future 
The following scenario is an attempt to describe the context in which the 2031 entertainment 
industry is embedded and should be taken with a grain of salt: 
 
As the automatization of jobs continues, more people feel less useful and struggle to participate in 
society through their profession. Creating meaning in life and simply being occupied and content is a 
harder task for the individual (Harari, 2017). People may have more free time (Dolton, 2017) (especially 
in the Nordics), but this doesn’t mean that meaning will be automatically added to their life. The 
development of VR and AR creates an extended reality, where people can spend their free time, work, 
create and collaborate in new ways. People may socialize more in increasingly sophisticated virtual 
worlds (Forbes, 2020), but the quality of the interactions wouldn’t live up to the ones of physical 
interactions. The entertainment industry is making use of these new technologies, but it is doubtful to 
what degree it makes people feel more connected. Loneliness will continue to affect everyone, also 
young people. After COVID-19, more people asked to work from home (European Commission, 2020), 
a tendency facilitated by the development of 5G. Combined with the fact that more people live alone, 
this is now lead to people being less used to socialize in real life. Truly social spaces – open-minded 
spaces where people can socialize in real life – are not very widespread. Consequently, this future is 
populated by individuals feeling more disconnected from each other, less used to socialize in real life, 
and struggling to find a sense of purpose, in a world living the consequences of climate change. 
 
We located this scenario in the bottom left 
quadrant of our future context. The likelihood of 
this scenario will indeed grow if people react 
passively to this development (in the consumer 
side of the spectrum), and if their immediate 
individual needs leads their choices more than 
what could be better for society at large in the 
long run.

Desired future 
The following scenario is an attempt to describe the context in which the 2031 entertainment 
industry is embedded and should be taken with a grain of salt: 
The extended reality created by the VR and AR technology of 2031 is used for creative and social 
purposes. Artists are collaborating from different continents in VR (inspired by the interview with 
Meghan Rutigliano), and it is used as an open-source platform for prototyping for a better future. 
Climate change will be tackled more efficiently since nations will ally their forces and make use of the 
newest technology for this purpose. People are meaningful in all areas of life – at work, but also in 
relationships, friendships, hobbies. The importance of this basic human has been highlighted by 2031’s 
recent advances in neurology and human sciences. An increased environmental conscience, combined 
with attractive, sustainable solutions (so-called “hedonistic sustainability”(Ingels, 2011)), is forging a 
more collectivistic mindset. Because of this, individual self-development – now a top priority for an 
increasing amount of people - interferes less with collective concerns, as it did in the last decade. Work 
and private life are more merged (Morisson, 2019); working hours and workspaces are more flexible, 
and creative co-working spaces with refined interiors are now the norm in the Nordics. The human need 
for social connections will be recognized, and it will be prioritized when designing or redesigning urban 
areas, businesses, physical and virtual spaces. Industrial areas will be used as entertainment districts 
at night, solving conflicts between stakeholders in cities (inspired by the interview with Steven Ra$pa) 
and unlocking new spaces for entertainment for an 
affordable 
price. 
A 
denser 
and 
more 
environmentally friendly mobility grid is being 
developed, facilitating transportation to and from 
these areas. Cities will experience a rise of tolerant 
and inclusive social spaces encouraging people to be 
themselves. 
We located this scenario in the top right quadrant of 
our future context. The likelihood of this scenario 
will indeed grow if people are more engaged in 
where the world is heading towards (in the creator 
side of the spectrum) and if they are able to see the 
bigger 
picture 
in 
their 
everyday 
actions 
(collectivistic side).  
Figure 8: Desired future 
Image 12: Visualization of Singapore Tower by BIG and CRA (Inhabitat, n.d.) 
==PAGE:58==
 
58 
 
The future scenarios are based on objective trends and developments that we have gathered through 
our research. However, the ViP-method allowed us to follow our intuition when deciding which aspects 
we considered relevant for the future of city entertainment. The two presented scenarios, as well as 
the projected impact of technology, are therefore tainted with some subjectivity. This allowed us as 
designers to communicate clearly our vision of the world in the domain of city entertainment. ViP 
provides the necessary framework to navigate between intuition and solid argumentation (Hekkert & 
Van Dijk, 2011).  
In addition to the two proposed future scenarios, we have also investigated what role technological 
development will play in 2031: 
 
 
 
Impact of technology 
Technology will have an increasingly deep impact on everyone in all fields of society. More jobs will be 
automated; in a global report from 2017, McKinsey & Company considers half of the current work 
activities to be automatable with the current technology. They predict that 400 million workers (15% 
of the world’s workforce) could potentially be replaced by 2030 because of automation (McKinsey 
Global Institute, n.d.). More people may have access to immersive virtual experiences. But 
technological development until now has shown us that it comes with a crippling number of 
undesirable side-effects. For example, it is now proved that heavy use of social media has a strong 
link with depression, anxiety, loneliness, self-harm, and even suicide (Orlowski, 2020). 
A European is checking his cell phone an average of 41 times a day (Pope, n.d.) and an American 96 
times (“Americans Check Their Phones 96 Times a Day,” 2019) – artists are starting to ban cell phone 
usage at music concerts (CNN, 2020). We have defined this evolution as Distractive Technology. Cell 
phones and the overwhelming accessible information has shortened our attention span, and the online 
world is plagued with unavoidable advertisement.  We have defined this evolution as Overwhelming 
Technology. However, technology can also empower people and boost society at large and in a more 
subtle way. Indoor climate and offered services of venues can be updated in real-time with sensors 
and intelligent use of data about people movements.  Wearable tech, such as smart RFID wristbands 
can be used for cashless payments and to exchange contact information with one another at events 
(Social Tables, 2020). We have called this development Discrete Technology. These same bracelets can 
feature an LED and are all synced – making its owner feel part of the show (CrowdSync Technology, 
n.d.), and can be used as tools to gamify events. Projector mapping can create immersive environments 
without the use of screens. We have called this development Supportive Technology. The figure below 
shows how we predict the development of technology, according to the different futures we have 
described. We advocate for a future of the entertainment industry with discrete and supportive 
technology.  
Image 13: Immersive art installation in the Digital Art Museum in Tokyo (whereintokyo.com, n.d.) 
==PAGE:59==
 
59 
 
We have now set the stage for two possible future scenarios, and how technology plays a role in these. 
The most likely one isn’t satisfactory according to our criteria. People seem more lonely, less fulfilled, 
and are passive victims of global warming. Our design should help in transitioning from the likely future 
to the desired future. 
We have described what could affect city entertainment in 2031. With this in mind, we are now going 
to formulate our statement, stating what we want to achieve with our design intervention. 
 
 
Figure 9: Impact of technology 
 
 
 
Figure 10: Our design intervention will help to transition from the likely future to the desired future 
==PAGE:60==
 
60 
 
 
4. Statement definition 
 
The statement makes the end goal explicit and opens-up new opportunities. Formulating the statement 
is choosing a direction, involving taking a position in supporting or fighting the world we see. In the 
previous chapter, we described the likely and the desired future. In this step, we act and address the 
desired future and what we want to achieve. 
Taking a position 
The ViP-method encourages one to take gut feeling and intuition into account when choosing a 
direction and making a critical decision. At the same time, each decision should be justified by a sound 
argumentation. The designer should also strive to understand the origin and the consequences of each 
decision. We want to encourage the “creator” behavior rather than the “consumer” behavior. Most 
businesses and capitalism, in general, seem to encourage us to consume more (e.g., through an 
advertisement), yet the encouragement for “creation” seems to be more marginal. Furthermore, 
mindless consumption is unsustainable and accelerates the depletion of the earth’s resources (Bohler, 
2019). In interactive physical entertainment - where the activity is instigated by the user - consumption 
is not the core activity. For us, it is the quality of the social interactions that our intervention affords 
that is important, not what can be consumed there. We also decided to design for people who are 
oriented towards others rather than oriented towards themselves. To combat loneliness in cities, it 
seems intuitive to design for this mindset. While popular self-help books advocate that the solution 
for loneliness lies within oneself (Oldenburg, 1989), we believe that loneliness is to be fought together. 
In addition, promoting community-centered behavior resonates with the core Nordic values of Trust 
and Compassion (The Nordics, n.d.). This suggests that a Nordic city such as Copenhagen could be a 
viable place for our design intervention. 
Tackling disconnection 
Based on our research, one of the problems we can see today that might grow in the proposed likely 
future is the disconnection between oneself and the world. In this scenario, people are more 
individualistic and caught up in consumption, fueling an unsustainable treadmill of supply and demand 
(Bohler, 2019). This could be due to a capitalized world, where we perceive that many things are 
centered around money and profit. We would like to bring back a healthy balance into our lives. A 
balance between our authentic self, our social circle, and our society dreaming of a shared 
epistemology. To achieve this goal, we must address several parts of our socio-cultural system 
together. We are investigating what it means to be connected to oneself as an individual or within a 
community.  
The term collectively connected, was crafted to describe the desired experience of our design 
intervention. The ViP method encourages indeed to create new words when needed, to overcome the 
limits of language (Hekkert & Van Dijk, 2011). We defined collectively connected as the superposition 
of three individually perceived connections: connected with oneself, connected with others, and 
connected to the globalized world. The concept is illustrated by the Venn diagram below. 
==PAGE:61==
 
61 
 
 
The following definitions are subjective and simplified - they are inspired by findings from our research: 
Connected with oneself: Being in touch with one’s 
emotions, values, and body. One is present and 
grounded in the moment. One can express your 
authentic self. 
Connected with others: Sharing authentically one’s 
emotions, values and engaging in the same actions or 
activities with other people. 
Connected to the globalized world: Feeling part of one 
complex ecosystem (the world’s fauna, flora, and 
geology) and feeling responsible that one’s actions are 
affecting the world, being able to see universal 
similarities in all humans on earth.  
Our statement is, therefore 
 the following: 
 
 
Creating our own word to define connection proved to be extremely beneficial. The concept of 
collectively connected provides a framework to tackle a complex socio-technical problem on three 
different levels: the individual, the social network, and the global society. Disconnection can now be 
considered in a holistic way, opening up opportunities for more appropriate design solutions.  
The importance of behavior 
To achieve social impact, the vision has to initiate behavioral change (Tromp & Hekkert, 2016). In 
Designing For Society (2018), Tromp & Hekkert argue that it is only when people put things in practice 
in the world that they change the world. This is achieved through behaviors, defined as “anything that 
affects others and/or the planet”(Tromp & Hekkert, 2018). For example, using a car-sharing app is a 
behavior - ceasing to do something or not doing something at all is also a behavior. On the other hand, 
“feeling responsible for global warming” is not a behavior since it is not translated into actions in the 
world. Therefore, our statement had to be translated into concrete behavioral goals, leading to the 
desired social implications. The latter describes the change to collective behavior that affects the state 
of society (Tromp & Hekkert, 2018). However, as we have defined it, “Feeling collectively connected” 
is not a collective behavior but a collective state of mind – it describes a feeling instead of actions in 
the real world. It would, therefore not be a social implication according to Trump & Hekkert’s 
definition. “Feeling collectively connected was therefore translated into concrete social implications. 
We analyzed what core behaviors each three aspects of collectively connected contained and what 
social implications were wished. Appendix B.3 describes how we made this transition. 
“We want people to feel collectively connected while they 
are engaged in an event.” 
Figure 11: connected with oneself, connected with 
others and connected to the globalized world 
==PAGE:62==
 
62 
 
  
Overcoming clashes of concern 
A problem this statement faces is that long-term collective interests often clash with short-term 
individual interests. For example, talking to one’s neighbor in the local supermarket strengthens the 
neighborhood cohesion (long-term collective concern) but one may avoid the interaction to save time 
in one’s busy day (short-term individual concern) (Tromp & Hekkert, 2018). This is qualified as a social 
dilemma. A strategy to overcome it is by making the desired pro-social behavior meaningful for the 
user at the same time. After critical examination of the multiple conflicting concerns, we designed 
mechanisms that will make the behavior relevant for the user. The table below show the identified 
individual and societal concerns for the desired behavior “talking more honestly about their values and 
emotions”. Tables describing the identified individual and societal concerns for the other desired 
behaviors can be found in appendix B.4. 
 
 
Individual 
Society 
Long term 
 
+ Cohesion 
+ Increased empathy 
+ More conscious 
population 
+ Mental health 
Short term 
 
- Uncomfortable 
- Strange  
- Vulnerable 
+ Stress Relief 
+ Reflecting 
 
Table 1: Individual and societal concerns for the desired behavior: 
 "talking more honestly about one’s values and emotions.” 
 
We arrived at the conclusion that in a gathering, people like to express themselves differently and to 
engage in different activities based on their mood. We believe that the users should not feel forced to 
do or feel anything specific. Making people feel in a specific way is a very ambitious task and making 
someone feel collectively connected seems even harder. We know from our context factors that 
everyone has a different way of experiencing something. So, we want to allow users to feel however 
they want and empower them to express this authentically. A principle to keep in mind while designing 
for collective freedom and self-expression is the following: “You are allowed to do anything as long as 
you do not disturb other people’s experience” This is inspired by an early implicit rule of Burning Man, 
told to us by Harley Dubois (co-founder of Burning Man). If we can create room for self-expression, 
==PAGE:63==
 
63 
 
that will by itself be a catalyzer for genuine interactions between people. We believe this will make 
users feel collectively connected.  
Below are described the desired social implications, the associated behaviors we want to initiate and 
the mechanism giving this behavior meaning for the user. The full analysis can be found in Appendix 
B.4.  At this stage of the process, the mechanisms are still quite abstract, in order not to be fixed on a 
specific type of design intervention. Tables featuring the final mechanisms can be found in Appendix 
B.4 and B.5. We decided to prioritize the goals with a socializing purpose before the others, as this is 
the most fundamental part of the vision.  
 
 
Primary goal: Increased social capital 
This is the main focus of our design intervention. Social capital is defined by sociologist Pierre Bourdieu 
as “the networks of relationships among people who live and work in a society, enabling that society 
to function effectively” (Bourdieu & Wacquant, 1992). When making decisions later in the design 
process, these two desired effects will be prioritized.

We have now clearly defined the desired social implications, the behavioral changes that are needed 
to achieve these social implications and the mechanisms to initiate those behavioral changes. Starting 
with defining the desired changes in society helped us stay true to our vision.  
This was the final step of Part 1: designing the future context. We have generated a large amount of 
context factors, which we clustered in 8 categories, each describing an aspect of our perception of the 
future of city entertainment: The forgotten importance of rituals; Simultaneously looking for a tribe 
and trying to be unique; The new age of experimental underground events; The entertainment industry 
is a reflection of the desires of society; A need for meaningfulness;, The online and offline interference; 
Loneliness and anxiety; Everyone can become an artist.  
Because our clusters seemed to refer to underlying opposing forces, we placed our them in a polar 
system with distinct dimensions. A matrix was created, outlining the event-goers’ attitude towards 
==PAGE:65==
 
65 
 
leisure time and towards society. This was used as a basis to create 2 future scenarios: a likely and a 
desired future – each describing how 2031 would look like in different corners of the matrix. The 
potential impacts of climate change, the development of AI and technological development in the 
entertainment industry were then discussed. We chose to design for the behaviors “collectivistic” and 
“creator”, as it was judged to be a more sustainable approach, and because current society didn’t seem 
to encourage the “creator” behavior, that we believe lies inside each of us. We decided to tackle 
disconnection, as it was found to be a major problem in our likely future. To better understand the 
complexity of the problem, we defined the term “collectively connected”, describing disconnection 
with oneself, with others and with the world. We defined our statement as following: “We want to 
make people feel collectively connected when they are engaged in an event”. To ensure social impact, 
our statement was converted into social implications (what collective change of behavior we wish to 
initiate with our design intervention), behavioral changes (needed to achieve the desired social 
implications) and the design mechanisms (needed to initiate those behaviors).  
 
Conclusion 
The next step is to describe the desired relationship between the user and the object. This is the topic 
of the next part of the ViP process, where we will describe the interaction level of the product. 
 
 
==PAGE:66==
 
66 
 
Part 2: Interaction 
5. Establishing a relationship: Designing human-product interaction 
 
In this step we are on the interaction level of the product. The interaction describes how the to-be-
designed object is related to the user and vice versa. We are going to present a systematic approach 
we used to identify these characteristics of the interaction. The difficulty of this task is to see what kind 
of interaction will fulfill the desired outcome of the established statement (we want people to feel 
collectively connected while they are engaged in a physical event). The interaction qualities are like a 
bridge between the statement and the concept. Each one of them is like a channel where a very specific 
information/experience travels from the product to the user and vice versa. 
Finding the appropriate analogy 
How can the relationship between the users and our design be described? The right interaction can be 
defined and expressed in many ways. It can be words, images, drawings, movie clips or analogies 
(Hekkert & Van Dijk, 2011). First, we were looking at analogous situations that can describe the 
relationship between the design and the user accurately. Analogous situations can be one those in 
which people interact with other products, artefacts, or other people  (Hekkert & Van Dijk, 2011). A 
good analogy is tangible, and fits to our statement, conveying the feeling of connectively connected. 
After trying out different analogies, we found a cacao ceremony to be adequate. 
 
Analogy: A Cacao Ceremony 
A cacao ceremony is a ritualized event where the participants consume a sacred cacao drink. The idea 
comes from native tribes who give high significance and spiritual meaning to certain plants. A 
ceremony is usually held with a higher intention in mind, with a group of people who do not necessarily 
Figure 12: (School of Embodied Leadership — HAEIN YOGA STUDIO . Yoga & Holistic Health, n.d.) 
==PAGE:67==
 
67 
 
know each other. To be able to go through this ritual they must establish some groundwork and make 
everyone feel collectively connected (Cacao Ceremony – Pure Kakaw, n.d.).  
The results of interaction qualities in this exercise came from our subjective reflection based on 
personal experience, together with the description of such an event from an informal interview. Using 
the method of ViP, further described in detail in Appendix B.6, the ceremony analysis led us to identify 
these following. We identified six interaction qualities: solemn, physical, reliable, encouraging, playful 
and uncomfortable.  
Solemn: 
A feeling of special importance which creates a unique atmosphere. We believe it will help to create 
cohesion between the users. 
Physical: 
Human touch and physical contact have many health benefits. This quality expresses the presence in 
body and the presence in mind. 
Reliable: 
Reliability is strongly linked with physical and mental safety. In order to surrender and enjoy the 
experience, no energy should be spent on evaluating risks or consequences. 
Encouraging: 
To thrive as an individual or as a group, encouragement in reaching your goals is fundamental. To feel 
connectively connected, a positive push will help to overcoming related doubts and fears. 
Playful: 
We believe we all have a desire and a need to play throughout our life. From our research and what 
we have observed adults tend to overthink things, and many are disconnected from their “inner child”. 
Overthinking means one only exists in one’s head which eventually prevents him/her from engaging 
with his/her/their environment. Playfulness helps to bring out our inner child and makes complex tasks 
into fun exercises. Bringing playfulness into the interaction helps one to be more present and more 
curious. A good example is how easily kids can engage with each other. They are more innocent and 
easier find it easier to make friends. We want to think about our users as big kids and help them to feel 
this way so they can lower their boundaries and start natural conversations. 
Uncomfortable: 
This may sound contradictory to what we are trying to make, however the idea behind it is innovative. 
In an interview with us, Harley Dubois argued that putting people in slightly uncomfortable situations 
creates a need to overcome this uncomfortableness through personal effort. This shows the person’s 
dedication and his/her/their investment in the goal, and eventually leads to personal growth. Everyone 
experiencing this discomfort and managing to overcome it alone or together will contribute to feel 
connectively connected.  
 
==PAGE:68==
 
68 
 
The cacao ceremony is a good analogy because the interactions fulfill the goal of our statement (“We 
want people to feel collectively connected while they are engaged in an event.”), yet there are other 
analogies that can create the same effect. Sports and the gatherings organized around these events is 
something that have connected people since centuries. Football is unquestionably the most successful 
and famous among all of them. It is a sport that beyond any doubt connects people of all generations 
around the globe. However, the interactions are very much centralized and limited around the event 
itself and do not facilitate personal goals or mindfulness. 
Conclusion 
In this step we decided on the interaction qualities that fulfill the goal of the statement. For this we 
used a Cacao ceremony as an analogous situation where people feel collectively connected. We found 
six interaction qualities to fulfill the statement: solemn, physical, reliable, encouraging, playful, and 
uncomfortable. We are going to use these to develop our concepts further in the next step when we 
are defining the product qualities. 
 
 
==PAGE:69==
 
69 
 
Part 3: New Product 
 
In this last stage of the ViP process we will go into the product level of the concept. In the previous 
chapter we selected solemn, physical, reliable, encouraging, playful, and uncomfortable as interaction 
qualities that are meant to the users to feeling connectively connected in an event. In this chapter we 
are going to test them to see if they can fulfil this purpose. First, we create product qualities, and 
thereafter the concept ideas. 
6. Defining product qualities 
 
A product quality is a step further from the interaction qualities. For each interaction quality we 
identified and linked one or more product qualities. These product qualities are the physical or 
metaphorical attributes of the product which help to fulfill the desired interaction. These qualities are 
realized by the user during the use. (Hekkert & Van Dijk, 2011) In other words, defining the product 
qualities means creating the intended user experience.  
We have selected 15 product qualities: Ritualized, Meaningful, Atmospheric, Interactive, 
Materialized, Highly Customizable, Creative, Innocent, Honest, Consistent, Curiosity Awakening, 
Daring, Genuine, Supportive and Uplifting. They are either action related characteristic or humanistic 
attributes/characteristics. The table below summarizes them and gives our definition of their meaning 
on how to interpret them as we move on in the process. 
Interaction qualities 
Product qualities  
Definition 
Solemn 
Ritualized 
Certain actions carried out in a specific order which 
creates a subjective experience 
Meaningful 
Has a higher purpose 
Atmospheric 
An environment which creates a distinct mood 
within oneself 
Physical 
Interactive  
Ability to sense, touch and change something 
Real/materialized  
Present in physical form 
Playful 
Highly Customizable 
The ability of one to change something according to 
one’s liking 
Creative 
Ability to express yourself (ideas/imagination) 
Innocent 
Without prejudice or any ulterior motives  
Reliable 
Honest 
Transparent and sincere

Ability to be predictable based on its behavior 
Uncomfortable

Daring

Supportive

physically or emotionally

Ability to feel inspired and optimistic 
==PAGE:70==
 
70 
 
These product qualities are turned into product features in the next step of our process. We believe 
that with our diverse set of qualities we can design an object which can support the goal to feel 
collectively connected. Our list of product qualities is numerous, but it is important that these qualities 
might be realized on different levels of the object (Hekkert & Van Dijk, 2011). So, these product 
qualities are all fulfilling a different purpose. 
We have now defined the physical qualities our design intervention should embody in order to fulfill 
the desired interactions. Until now, we haven’t decided how our design intervention will manifest in 
the world. We have kept it open in order not to limit the solution space to a specific type of solution. 
The interaction and product qualities could characterize a product, a system, a governmental policy, 
guidelines, an event… In the next step, we will finally make the transition from qualitative 
characteristics to a tangible manifestation in the world, with features and properties. 
 
 
7. Concept design, or “concepting” 
 
Concepting is the translation of our vision into something tangible that can be literally perceived, used, 
and experienced. (Hekkert & Van Dijk, 2011) In this stage we are going to translate the statement, the 
interaction and product qualities into a product with tangible features and properties. Only at this stage 
we are going to decide what our final solution should be. 
Together, the context factors, the interaction- and product qualities form a manifestation. This usually 
happens naturally, emerged without effort from the accumulated knowledge. 
We considered 3 types of interventions described on the following pages. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:71==
 
71 
 
Neighbourhood day - New purposeful bank holidays 
 
Several days a year are set apart to 
neighbourhood life, creating the time that 
people don’t have in order to engage in 
these activities. 
During most of human history, people 
used to live in tight-knitted communities 
(Harari, 2014). This concept is an attempt 
to bring back this feeling of community, by 
connecting people that already live 
physically close – neighbours. Frequent 
interaction 
with 
neighbours 
will 
strengthen 
neighbourhood 
cohesion, 
create a feeling of community, and bring 
back a sense of belonging. The idea is to 
free time for these interactions to happen 
and to ritualize the process by creating dedicated days for it. Each block of flats will be asked to 
collaborate internally and make the content of the Neighbourhood Day. This is a concept taken from 
Burning Man, which advocates for the importance of engaging citizens in creating the cities that they 
want to live in (Raspa, 2019) and call for a return to neighbourliness (Farr, 2018). 
By providing some initial guidelines, and after enough time, traditions proper to each neighbourhood 
will emerge. People will be encouraged to collaborate over projects, make activities or hobbies 
together.  
Pros 
- 
Top-down changes are generally effective 
ways to initiate transformation (Beck, 
2020)  
- 
Requires little physical means. 
- 
Instead of being nudged to act in a 
specific way, the government is being 
very explicit about the desired effect. 
- 
Gives a lot of creative freedom to the 
users. 
- 
May increase the public popularity of the 
government that propose this measure. 
- 
The design intervention is well targeted 
(touchpoint: where people live) 
Cons 
- 
There is a high risk that people just chose 
to take a day off instead of engaging in 
neighbourhood communion. How can the 
municipality ensure that this isn’t 
happening? 
- 
It may take many years before any 
traditions emerge and that the desired 
effect may be visible. 
- 
Can be seen as unproductive for 
businesses, as it removes working days. 
- 
People can’t choose their neighbours (I.e. 
those they will be asked to spend more 
time with). 
Image 14: Conceptual representation of Neighborhood Days 
==PAGE:72==

Pits & Hills - Public space 
Mindfully designed public space, with different height levels facilitating different behaviors.  
The space is divided in areas facilitating different kind of interactions: approaching new people, being 
with known people, being on your own and experiencing something together. By helping urbanites to 
act as they feel, they should feel empowered and encouraged to dare talking with others. This follows 
the principle of “people prefer things to happen naturally”, that we uncovered while generating 
context factors. The space has some playful elements, such as swings and sunken trampolines. Some 
artifacts work as connection points to facilitate random interactions such as ping-pong tables. Half of 
the place is covered, and windshielded to ensure that it is used all year round.  
To enable change over time, and provide space for the unexpected, the place is designed to anchor 
temporary structures, and regularly hosts events. It follows the principles of the fixed, the flexible and 
fleeting described by Jan Gehl (2013) to allow self-expression, play and exercise in the city space. the 
fixed refers to the city space. the flexible are temporal facilities and events taking place on the space 
during the year (e.g., a funfair or a Christmas market) and the fleeting are minor events such as 
concerts or firework shows (Gehl, 2013).  
Pros 
- 
Very inclusive – it’s a public space so 
anyone can use it at any time 
- 
Positive local impact, direct benefits from 
residents in the area 
- 
Doesn’t require too many means 
- 
Gives value for the municipality 
- 
Follows the trends of making cities more 
people-friendly and agile 
Cons 
- 
Low degree of facilitation of positive 
random encounters 
- 
Since it’s an open public space, it is not 
regulated who uses the space. 
Undesirable behaviors can occur 
- 
Resistance requirements, no free 
elements  
- 
Not extremely innovative, cities are 
already implementing mindfully designed 
public space 
Image 15: Conceptual illustration of Pits & Hills 
==PAGE:73==
 
73 
 
Kairos - Privately owned place 
 
Kairos is a privately owned place 
where urbanites spend time for 
communing, celebrating and realizing 
themselves in their leisure time. It is 
financed by a monthly membership 
fee. 
Kairos is a hybrid between a cultural 
house and a day-club (a venue 
facilitating festive events during the 
day). Like shared offices and maker 
spaces, the users can deepen their 
interest in their hobby, but outside of 
their work. Social cohesion between 
urbanites 
is 
improved 
through 
engagement 
in 
activities. 
Users 
contribute to the place by proposing, 
participating and teaching the activities they wish. The word Kairos comes from Ancient Greek,  
and  signifies the right, critical, or opportune moment for action (Wikipedia, n.d.-b). This place 
encourages immediacy and helps users to be in the present moment in order to feel collectively 
connected. Kairos has a clear vision; where connecting people, being authentic while improving 
oneself and actively participating are core elements. It is governed by a list of principles inspired by 
Burning Man – adapted to privately-owned space in the city. Inspiration can be taken from the book 
Sustainable Nation (Farr, 2018), providing examples on how these principles can be applied to an urban 
context (p. 161).  
Over time, the vision evolves with the member’s input, and 
the principles accordingly. To include most nationalities, the 
official language is English.  
The collective forms a community, a “family” where people 
are constantly evolving and growing personally in an open-
minded and safe environment. The concept then turns into a 
new meaningful part of one’s life balancing out the “work-
private life interference” identified while structuring the 
future context.  
 
Figure 13: Kairos combines the facilitation of 3 activities: celebrating, community-building and self-realizing 
 
 
 
Image 16: Conceptual illustration of Kairos 
==PAGE:74==
 
74 
 
 
Pros 
- 
A vision and principles are non-material 
ways to connect people, and have been 
used efficiently through history. 
- 
Provides a new type of space currently 
missing in cities. 
- 
Creating a community seems to be the 
most resilient way to sustain regular 
meaningful interaction and to facilitate 
cooperation. 
- 
Adds a new meaningful part in the users’ 
lives. 
Cons 
- 
Requires more means than the other 
solutions. 
- 
More complex concept. 
- 
It may take time before the intended 
effects take place. 
- 
Since the place is reserved for members, 
it will require a high number of members 
if it’s effects should ripple out on society. 
- 
Mixed-used spaces, providing classes in 
the week and hosting events in the 
weekends, already exist in the city. 
 
We sensed that a physical space with a strong community would be the best suited type of 
intervention, the goal being “making people feel collectively connected, while engaging in a physical 
event”. It is probable that people will have more free time already in 2031 (Dolton, 2017), so providing 
more time with Neighborhood Day may not be the right type of intervention. We realized that the 
problem was merely a lack of truly social spaces, so designing such a space would be more appropriate 
and effective. In order to easily monitor the people and thereby ensuring a more resilient community 
experience, a institution with a membership requirement seemed more realistic than an open, public 
space. Therefore, we chose to further develop on Kairos. 
As we worked with our concept, we saw the potential to be innovative, while at the same time 
recognizing similarities to with existing products. To differentiate ourselves in the early stage of 
concepting, we carried out a detailed benchmarking, while using the Business model canvas to design 
the key elements of our concept, and thereby clearly differentiate ourselves from existing solutions.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:75==
 
75 
 
Business Model 
 
A high-level business plan was carried out for Kairos. A Business Model Canvas and a Value Proposition 
Canvas were filled out for this purpose. In this section, we will briefly cover the customer segment, 
revenue model and key elements of the value proposition. The Business Model Canvas, the Value 
Proposition Canvas and a list of Design Criteria can be found in Appendix C.  
Customer segment: 
ViP warns against predefining a customer segment, pointing out that it could limit the solution space. 
The reason is that what is holding these people together may not be a relevant element for the 
interaction with the desired solution (Hekkert & Van Dijk, 2011). Furthermore, focusing on a specific 
set of people may limit the reach of the solution, thereby limiting potential market penetration and 
success. We have therefore defined a very open target group, being anyone above 18 who is interested 
in the mindset behind the place. We are aiming to be inclusive with anyone who has the right mindset 
and wants to participate in making the concept better by any form of contribution. Our customer 
segment includes among others: people looking for a meaningful social interaction, alternative 
eventgoers, people aspiring to engage in alternative ways of living, people concerned by personal 
growth, expats and foreigners living in the city, artists, entertainers. 
 
Revenue model: 
Kairos is a non-profit organization. If it were to be created in Denmark, it would fall under the legal 
status of “Almindelig forening” (regular association). These associations are registered as non-profit 
business entities, with an annual turnover of more than 500 000 DKK (approx. 81 790 $ and 67 224 €) 
(Legal Desk, n.d.). Members are paying a monthly contribution, and the money for basic expenses, 
events and developing the place. Additionally, rooms are rented out for private and business usage.  
Unique values: 
- 
A place where everyone is treated as a unique individual, yet as part of a community. 
- 
Meaningful, highly refined and versatile environment with entertainment purpose. 
- 
Scientifically backed education of participants in mindfulness and towards better wellbeing. 
- 
An app which helps to maintain the communication of information such as economic 
transparency, info about events, and user- ability to give rating and feedback for improvement.  
- 
International environment where all information is communicated in English.  
- 
An active and engaging community who actively uses the space. 
- 
Support local businesses and emerging artists. 
Most values we identified are different from the majority of social spaces, such as clubs, bars or culture 
houses. This points towards a blue ocean (an ill-defined industry, where boundaries have not been 
defined and with no apparent competition (PowerMBA, n.d.)) in the domain of entertainment with 
socializing purposes.  
Below are key elements of the value proposition: 
==PAGE:76==
 
76 
 
Customer Jobs 
The customer job is twofold: Self-actualization through authentic self-expression and socializing 
outside of their home in their leisure time. “Authenticity, or the feeling of being true to oneself, is key 
to both well-being and employee engagement“ (Sutton, 2020). Real life socializing will reduce the time 
spent in front of screens and will eventually guide the individual from self-centered attention, towards 
attention to the surroundings. This increases the chances of strangers getting into random 
conversations and sharing some meaningful moments together. This will eventually increase the 
persons’ ‘empathic muscle’ and social skills (Greene, 2018). 
 
Customer Pains 
The pains of the customer are both physical and psychological. There is a fear that one is going to end 
up lonely in life, which is an unquestionable fear of all of us. Additionally, there is a pain in the lack of 
truly social third places which would foster community between urbanites. At the same time, even 
though smartphones have the potential to bring us together, “the electronic connection is not a valid 
substitute for face-to-face human contact”. “In truth, no substitute for human contact truly exists.” 
(Landin, 2019) 
Another pain is about executing payments frequently while one wants to engage in social activities in 
third places. This procedure interrupts the flow and the experience when one is concerned about one’s 
financial status. 
 
Gains Creators 
The gains by our unique value are a community of likeminded people and feeling of belonging - being 
part of a tribe. The concept provides a safe environment for self-expression which contributes to 
authenticity, growth, and self-actualization. Authenticity is becoming increasingly important in 
measuring economic and societal success; “creating the space and encouragement for diverse 
authentic expressions of self is therefore likely to have wide reaching positive impacts at work and in 
wider society.” (Sutton, 2020).  
 
Verifying the innovativeness: competitor analysis and benchmarking: 
 
In order to verify the originality of Kairos, places with seemingly similar functions were identified and 
analyzed. This helped us refine and validate the innovativeness of Kairos. Below is a summary of the 
process and main findings – a more detailed explanation can be found in Appendix D. 
We mapped out existing concepts in the area of Copenhagen from the same field, that we defined as 
places, events, or organizations where one would spend time for communing, celebrating, and realizing 
oneself. The purpose was to become aware of existing “third places” that would be inspiring and/or 
provoking. If the concept were to be realized in Copenhagen, these could potentially become direct or 
indirect competitors. 
We looked first at the places’ ability to enable the intended effect (in our case celebration, communing 
and self-realization), and only afterwards what type of place it is (Hekkert & Van Dijk, 2011). The result 
was a broad and heterogenous list of 64 interventions, spanning from bars to collaborative art studios. 
==PAGE:77==
 
77 
 
We identified fifteen places with an organization behind them, facilitating all three activities in an 
engaging way: communion, celebrations and possibility to realize oneself (through art, cultural and 
physical activity). These were defined as direct competition because of their similarity to Kairos. These 
15 places were then rated according to Kairos’ Design Criteria. The rating system was the following: 0 
for criteria not fulfilled, 1 for criteria fulfilled. The criteria with high importance were multiplied by 2, 
and the criteria with moderate importance were multiplied by 1. Below is an extract of the table 
displaying the 5 places that scored highest. The full table can be found in “F.4: Comparative Matrix “. 
Five places stood out form this comparison, and they were further analyzed. 
 
Table 2: Extract of the comparative matrix displaying the five places with the highest overall rating.

Figure 14: Our five main competitors 
Using the Blue Ocean Strategy, the competitive factors of these places were generated, among them 
“strong community”, “alternativeness” and “value for money” (see Appendix D.2, Comparative Matrix 
for full list). The concepts were then rated on how well they performed on each factor, and so was 
Kairos.  
Here are some of our main discoveries of the overall process: 
Copenhagen Municipality has a big network of Culture Houses, hosting musical events, dancing, 
theater and more. Many houses also offer various classes and workshops. A main player is DIT:KBH, 
composed of 12 cultural institutions supporting volunteering, co-creation and creative growth in 
Copenhagen. One of them, Huset KBH, features a boardgame café and a cinema and hosts 1500 events 
yearly (Velkommen Til DIT:KBH, n.d.). One main focus is on making culture accessible to the average 
citizen.  
Independent popular spaces, such as Bolsjefabrikken or Folkets Hus, scored higher on 
“alternativeness” and conveyed overall a higher feeling of authenticity. However, these places were 
seemingly less organized, and the integration process seemed more closed. For example on their 
website (only partially translated in English), Bolsjefabrikken informs that in order to make an event, 
one should befriend people first, then attend events and talk about one’s ideas ( 
 » Bolsjefabrikken, 
n.d.). Hygiene and safety don’t seem to be a primary concern of these places either.  
==PAGE:79==
 
79 
 
All these institutions are open to anyone, without any membership requirement.  Some events require 
an entrance fee. There is also usually a clear distinction between host (or content maker) and user. We 
didn’t find any institution with a mentoring system, where everyone is encouraged to teach and to 
learn something.  
This made us clear were we stood compared to the existing market, and led us to identify new 
competitive factors, such as “skill sharing” and “highly refined interior”. 
The Four Action Framework above shows in what aspects Kairos is different than existing similar 
solutions.  
Having refined and assessed the innovativeness of Kairos, we decided to continue with the concept of 
a hybrid place, Kairos. In the next chapter we will present the final iteration of our concept. This is the 
last step of the ViP-process, named Design and detailing.
