Chapter 5: 
Heterotopia  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:81==
 
81 
 
 
 
 
Figure 16 Aerial cross-section of Heterotopia 
CHAPTER 5 
==PAGE:82==
 
82 
 
In this section, we are introducing our design which we renamed to Heterotopia. The name comes from 
a concept elaborated by Michel Foucault describing “cultural, institutional, and discursive spaces that 
are somehow ‘other’: disturbing, intense, incompatible, contradictory, or transforming. Heterotopias 
are worlds within worlds, mirroring and yet upsetting what is outside (Wikipedia, n.d.-a), (Foucault, 
2004). We call the members of the community Heterotopians. 
5.1 Welcome to Heterotopia! 
 
Heterotopia is a visionary social space, containing everything an urbanite needs to feel fulfilled and 
which actively helps said urbanite in growing and maintaining a diverse social circle. Heterotopia is 
founded on a solid set of moral values and principles, such as creative self-expression, radical 
collaboration and transparent information flow - allowing the concept to be agile and resilient to 
change. The full list of Principles is detailed in depth in section 5.3.1.  
 
Heterotopia provides a reliable and playful space, with the necessary facilities in an honest, open-
minded, and motivating environment. The official language is English, yet there is no requirement to 
speak English fluently to become a member, as further described below. The members of Heterotopia 
form an inclusive community, called Heterotopians. The community welcomes every new member 
(read about Application procedure for membership in section 5.3.2) and integrates them one-by-one 
to its existing community so all members feel comfortable with each other at all time. To facilitate the 
integration, new members receive a personalized Welcoming Ceremony (section 5.3.4). The 
Welcoming Ceremony and the Principles together are helping to integrate everyone to the rest of the 
community mindfully.  
==PAGE:83==
 
83 
 
Our Heterotopia is in a redesigned warehouse – a space vast enough for growth and far enough from 
residential areas to be able to have high noise levels at any desired time. The place’s size depends on 
the current number of members; here we have detailed a space for approx. 500 members (the 
framework for growing from 20 to 500 members is explained in steps 5.2.1 and 5.2.2). The stunning 
interior, mixing nature and technology, grabs the attention and creates an elevated feeling (see list of 
spaces in Appendix E.4). In a world plagued with loneliness and disconnection Heterotopia brings back 
the lost sense of community into urban life. The unique culture of the place cultivates values actively 
such as altruism, collaboration, transparency, and creative self-expression. Heterotopia is trying to 
bring fulfilment and personal development into the Heterotopian’s life. For example, members can 
join the Master-Apprentice system (read more in section 5.3.9). It is a mentoring system that is actively 
sharing skills and knowledge. During working hours, several areas can be used as co-working spaces, 
however we encourage mindful and conscious computer usage for increased presence and 
involvement in activities. 
  
With a vision as stunning and developed as this, a healthy economy is a must to stand a chance as a 
business. The revenue model is developed to be transparent and allow the space to expand with the 
same rate as its member base grows naturally (read How to build Heterotopia? section 5.2 for details). 
All the members co-own the space and pay a membership fee. The majority of the fee goes to rent and 
basic expenses, and the rest is invested in ideas proposed by other Heterotopians to upgrade the space. 
All money flow and transactions are transparent and accessible for every member. In doing so, we 
create the feeling of ownership in the members by showing them what their contribution is spent on. 
Additional money can be earned from donations or renting out spaces for various activities, such as 
for photoshoots, video productions, teambuilding events, creative workshops, or other activities for 
non-members of the community (a framework for this would be complex and it is not part of this thesis 
document). However, the real worth of Heterotopia cannot be expressed in money because the main 
value are the people and the knowledge which cannot be capitalized. 
5.1.1 The three pillars of Heterotopia: 
Good Culture, Personal Development, & Social Impact 
Good Culture 
First, we would like to create a culture inside Heterotopia. We believe that culture is making things 
alive in this world. Heterotopia is not just a place. It is a lifestyle, it is a collective of people, an extended 
family. Therefore, we find it essential to have a healthy culture in Heterotopia. Good culture for us is 
something that can live up to the challenges our civilization is facing at the moment and tackle them. 
In Heterotopia, we want people to be more collectively connected than what is currently offered (see 
Likely Future in Future Context section). The culture that we want to create is rooted in the forgotten 
importance of rituals (context factor cluster). We assume that rituals used to be more important, and 
they had a bigger impact on one’s life than today. That is why we want to introduce new meaningful 
rituals in Heterotopia. We see rituals as the binding material of a culture, so the community and 
togetherness are in the center of it. That is why rituals and celebrations are at the center of the culture 
of Heterotopia.  
 
 
==PAGE:84==
 
84 
 
Life not so long ago was full of celebrating and rituals, but these were removed them for the sake of a 
more efficient way of working (School Of Life, 2016). For example, “prior to 1834, the Bank of England 
observed about 33 saints' days and religious festivals as holidays, but in 1834, this was reduced to just 
four (LEOnetwork, n.d.)”, the argument being “the sooner such festivals and holy days were brought 
under control and reduced in number, the better for commerce, civic prosperity and genuine piety.” 
(Santino & Schmidt, 1996). This message underestimates thousands of years of human evolution, that 
were centered around community and deeply relying on each other. ‘Hunter-gatherers used to 
celebrate and hold rituals together at special occasions, where they shared their stories and exchange 
information. These gatherings might have been the catalysis of spreading knowledge about agriculture 
across the globe’ This message underestimates thousands of years of human evolution which was 
always centered around community and deeply relying on each other. ‘Hunter-gatherers used to 
celebrate and hold rituals together at special occasions. Here they shared their stories and exchanged 
information with each other. These gatherings might have been the catalysis of spreading knowledge 
about agriculture across the globe’ (Kurzgesagt - In a Nutshell, n.d.). If gatherings used to foster events 
so crucial in the evolution of consciousness, why are we not practicing it today (in a more sophisticated 
sense)? At Heterotopia we are arguing that rituals are the most fundamental ways of celebrating and 
gathering on meaningful occasions. Though we are not hunter-gatherers, anyone we need these 
occasions to gather and exchange knowledge with each other and feel connected to “our tribe.” These 
events are a time for celebration with a meaningful purpose, keeping connected to others, or sharing 
and exchanging knowledge in our fast-paced world.  
 
Personal Development 
The second pillar is what we believe is one of the most important aspect of everyone’s life, personal 
development. We think that in a good environment everyone can naturally evolve and grow as a 
person. What defines a good environment for us? It is inclusive: that is why we integrate every member 
one by one. It is diverse: we want to attract a diverse crowd of people to Heterotopia.  It is open minded 
and curious towards discovering, discussing and implementing new ideas. Because of this, a good 
environment is always evolving just like the members of the community. That is why Heterotopia is 
built on the three pillars (Good Culture, Personal Development, & Social Impact) and the Principles. 
The personal development (mostly self-actualization) is what we want to offer every Heterotopian by 
becoming a member and joining the community. That is why we encourage everyone to gain 
knowledge and share that knowledge in their field of interest by joining the master-apprentice system. 
This can foster collaborations within and outside of the borders of Heterotopia. This way contributing 
to welfare wellbeing and hopefully the spread of a mindset that Heterotopia wants to communicate. 
The strong community created by heterotopia also serves this goal. As discussed in the literature 
review, more and better social connection open up more economical opportunities; from buying a 
house to getting a job (Esteban & Roser, n.d.), which will propel personal development even further. 
 
Social Impact 
The third pillar is social impact. We want to create a behavior change that starting from Heterotopia 
ripples down on society and has a positive impact. This is the motivation and vision behind Heterotopia, 
and we believe the combination of elements that make up the concept can facilitate this change.  
 
These elements are introduced separately and detailed further in the following chapters.  
==PAGE:85==
 
85 
 
The Main Spaces of Heterotopia 
 
The visualization above illustrates the floor plan with the layout of the rooms. In our design proposal, 
the place (ground floor) contains the following areas. The central area is the Main Hall, the Event 
Horizon is in the middle of it in front of the bay window. It is the meeting point of Heterotopians. There 
is also an Art Arena in the Main Hall which is an ever-changing exhibition space. A complete floor plan 
can be found in Appendix E.3.

==PAGE:86==
 
86 
 
5.2 How to build Heterotopia? 
5.2.1 Approach 
Wanting to be idealistic while staying realistic in our goals, we saw that designing using an incremental 
approach seemed like the most logical way to approach the problem/design intervention. The reason 
for this was that the final product of what was now to be designed, was not just a place of gathering, 
but a complex ecosystem consisting of hundreds (potentially thousands) of individuals meant to thrive 
and coexist happily. By designing the concept carefully in stages, scaling gradually from 20 to 50, 150 
to finally 500 people, it helped both to uncover pitfalls, changes in dynamics and mindful transitions 
into bigger systems. To have a big impact, we saw not just the value for society if being able to scale, 
but the importance of starting with and maintaining a solid foundation as the concept starts bringing 
in more people. 
The design of multiple iterations, scaling incrementally in size and complexity was viewed from our 
perspective as a kind of an evolution of our solution. It seemed like the most logical way to design 
holistically and carefully, mindfully implementing what we had learned so far throughout our research, 
step by step.  
5.2.2 Evolving step by step 
 
Using the design criteria (Appendix C.4), we started out with concepting by listing all desired 
interactions we wanted to include into the concept of 20 people. Moving on, we sorted them by must-
have, could have, should have, nice to have (MoSCow-method), before we turned those interactions 
into activities. These activities were then mindfully arranged in a space, in such a way that each desired 
interaction would cause the least amount of interference for each other. An example like dancing 
(loud) and relaxing (calm) were to be placed the furthest away from each other to limit the disturbance 
of/incompatibility with each other. The amount of space itself was then calculated in terms of an MVP 
(minimum viable product), to see roughly how big of a place was required to fit the minimum desired 
interactions/criteria, while being cost-efficient and realistic. When successfully developed and run at 
a small scale, this would be our “pilot project” proving the first stage of the evolution of the concept. 
 
As we proceeded to develop further the concept of 20 people, towards the concept of 500, it continued 
to increase in complexity. The process involved rethinking, reflecting, iterating and evaluating both 
desired size and activities, purposed safety requirements, rules & principles, but also considering social 
structures like essential staff and volunteers, and importantly, the business model of how to run the 
place. With a heavy focus on organic growth, the concept of Heterotopia is more like a structure and 
framework for spaces to be organically designed and expanded in, rather than a single place for a 
concept. Stemming from the concept of organic growth, we considered the following to be a fitting 
metaphor for our concept’s development:  We were starting small, by planting a single seed meant to 
naturally adapt to the environment in which it is placed.  If nourished mindfully by its environment, 
the “plant” we call Heterotopia can have the necessary space and resources to grow to whatever size 
it makes sense to reach and maintain. Based on its environment, driven by the desire and imagination 
==PAGE:87==
 
87 
 
of its participants and members, they can contribute what they want to make a reality. In this sense, 
the space that makes Heterotopia is meant as a kind of “democratized dream manifestation machine”.  
5.2.3 Business revenue model 
To root our concept – our seed - in reality, we were looking into multiple businesses for inspiration, 
feasibility and validation. Among these were “Coop Norge SA” and “Waking up” while our main 
inspiration were “charity: water”, “Patron” and “Indiegogo” (for more info see Appendix C.1). We 
decided that what made the most sense was to be a non-profit organization, using its revenue to invest 
in itself to expand. 
The desired system/platform that we had in mind to achieve this goal would aim to fulfil the following 
criteria:  
Members should with ease: 
- 
Access all personal data 
- 
Create projects. 
- 
Influence the distribution of income/resources according his/her interest 
- 
See where their contribution goes specifically 
- 
Access all transactions made by the organization 
 
Transparency 
 
Inspired by charity water’s business model of 
dividing the revenue into two accounts (shown 
above), we decided that the coverage of all 
operational costs (like rent, electricity and payment 
for staff etc.) would go to one account, while the 
money that would be invested in the place would 
go to a separate one. Dividing the cost of the place 
into two separate accounts would make the 
members 
differentiate 
easier 
in-between 
fundamentally necessary costs, and improving, 
helpful and fun ones. Mainly, the idea here was to make the sensitive topic of money more transparent 
and easier to relate to, giving the contributor the understanding of what is necessary and what is 
optional. As described in “Transparency room/ L.A.R.S.” below, this is a purposed way of giving the 
user an easily digestible overview. Here we show that 100% of the membership fee goes to what is 
necessary to run the place.

Just like the image from “charity: water” (fig. 18, previous page) shows, these are all values we relate 
to. The purposed interface “L.A.R.S.”, would help keep the place democratized, transparent, trackable, 
and accessible in development and operation. L.A.R.S. would be a so-called “Transparency room”, 
where anyone would be able to access all current information about the economy, transactions, status 
of current and upcoming projects, current expenses and so on. This would heighten the motivation 
and trust of the community, seeing a direct impact of their contribution.  
==PAGE:89==
 
89 
 
Membership 
Being a member means that you have applied, been admitted and are thereby interested in supporting 
the place through participation and financial support for the minimum required to keep the place alive 
(at its current state/size). Meanwhile, wanting to be as inclusive as possible, we also encourage other 
members who can afford more, to pay for those who cannot. We want to put a big focus on the bare 
minimum, which means that the more members there are, the less everyone pays (based on current 
expenses). When infrastructure is upgraded, this is done shared or collectively, based on the 
supporters behind it.  
For the sake of risk-management, when it comes to the amount of monthly support from each 
member, “locking in” on a fixed amount/ “subscription” is what seems most realistic. This will be paid 
quarterly or yearly, using the same kind of structure and rules as renting a flat, with up to 3 months 
notification in advance of any changes in the members' ability to contribute occurs. In this way, 
Heterotopia as an organization has time to act accordingly.

• 
“There were 6,455,080 worldwide crowdfunding campaigns last year. (2020)”

• 
“The crowdfunding market is projected to grow to $300 billion by 2030.” 
-(Sheperd, 2019) 
What we desired to create was a highly transparent co-owned organization, with a focus on delivering 
value to all participants and members, creating Incentive and trust for the members to invest in the 
organization and thereby invest in themselves, their projects and their dreams. “On Patreon, you can 
let your fans become active participants in the work they love by offering them a monthly membership.” 
(Patreon, n.d.)

5.2.4 Conclusion 
Scaling and the revenue model are closely linked, so starting small and aiming high, we saw that for 
the final iteration we would tackle here, we decided that stopping at a 500 people-place was a good 
number. The reasoning was that it was big enough to prove a vast enough number of desired 
interactions in a big enough space while seeming economically feasible and not too complicated. Going 
further than this might have too many factors for us to consider for the sake of the duration and 
feasibility of this project, as a lot of the required information was accessible to businesses only, and 
entirely not possible to get a complete realistic overview of costs in time. Still, if the pilot project proves 
to works at this scale, using the concept of planned organic growth, it is also hard to see why it wouldn’t 
be able to reach as high as its members would desire, as long as its principles, culture, and transparency 
are kept intact. However, we recognize that our concept's future requires further research, iteration, 
and validation from experts in the topic such as economy and sociology.  
 
==PAGE:93==
 
93 
 
5.3 What makes Heterotopia so great? 
 
In this section, we detail out every touchpoint according to the desired behavior goal(s) they are 
supposed to fulfil. The aim is to provide mechanisms which help to successfully realize the visionary 
concept and its societal implications. To make this happen we must take into account every human as 
a complex creature with their individual fears and desires – concerns – and design mechanisms to help 
overcome them. For every desired effect, we mapped out the individual’s short-term concerns 
together with the long-term consequences on society.  As these are often conflicting, we looked at all 
of them individually (read more about the behavior goals and its relation to personal and societal 
concerns in Appendix B.4.). We discovered that people are mostly concerned about the following 
(based on our findings): 
• 
Social anxiety – one does not know how to behave or engage in a new context. 
• 
Energy input – it is energy-consuming to be social. 
• 
Lack of time – one only spends time on things one values. 
• 
Accessibility – it is too time-consuming to access certain things either physical or digital. 
• 
Motivation – lack of motivation and support from one’s environment to change. 
• 
Comfort/safety – the more novel or unfamiliar something is, the higher the perceived risks. 
• 
Overthinking – one tends to analyze everything for the worst outcome to avoid 
disappointment or failure. 
• 
Productivity – having leisure time is not considered as a “productive activity”.  
With this in mind, we started the design process. To mindfully follow our method, we included the 
relevant interaction and product qualities to ensure our successful design in all the 12 touchpoints we 
selected. We identified these touchpoints to be either a space, or an interaction which evokes 
emotion(s) in the user. These are places where people feel most vulnerable. To overcome their inner 
barriers, we designed powerful mechanisms to facilitate their transition. Eventually these mechanisms 
would help us to make the designed behavior change and the social implications within the concept of 
Heterotopia (read more about the touchpoints and their mechanisms in Appendix E.1). We detailed 
the touchpoints of the concept, together with the behavior goal it fulfills and by which mechanisms in 
(read more about the Appendix E.1.) 
Designing part 1: Future Context statement definition 
The following elements of the concepts will now be described in the next section: The Principles, the 
Connection Medallion, The Welcoming Ceremony, The enTrance, The Main Hall & Event Horizon, The 
Lounge, The Jungle Room, Atelier and Workshop Spaces, The Rave Cave, Disconnection Rooms, and 
the Bass Therapy Room. 
The elements of Heterotopia are explained by the aspects of ‘idea in’ and ‘idea with’ of the concept. 
The ‘idea in’ deals with the technical aspects, and the desired functions, while the ‘idea with’ deals 
with the context of use, social importance and need satisfaction(Andreasen et al., 2015). The concept 
descriptions give insight into the activity and the organ domains and do not include the part domain 
level of the concept. The activity domain focuses on how the product is used (drilling machine: how it 
is used for drilling holes). The organ domain focuses on what the product can do, i.e. how the functional 
elements interact to create the necessary effect(s) for the activity (drilling machine: motor, 
transmission, and clutch transmit force). (Andreasen et al., 2015). We used these tools because they 
are flexible to use and helpful in detailing more abstract conceptual parts of the design. 
==PAGE:94==
 
94 
 
5.3.1 Principles 
These principles are the byproduct of natural growth, maturing and refining the vision. Consequently, 
we cannot foresee what principles are needed in the future to remind people of the desired behavioral 
goals. The principles are inspiration for one on how to cultivate the mindset of Heterotopia and 
reminders on how one should act in any situation. But it will be always carrying a unique and 
personalized meaning to everyone who feels connected to them. We want to support the members in 
overcoming their short-term individual concerns and guide them towards the desired behavior 
beneficial for society. Because of this, the principles must feel meaningful and important. To elevate 
their importance, they are partially ritualized and their mantra-like repetitive usage will help the 
members act according to them. It creates a special atmosphere what we described as solemn and it 
is, in our opinion, one of the most important interaction qualities.  
We were inspired by the principles of Burning Man. These principles are guidelines for its participants, 
for integration, overcoming concerns and thinking in a novel way – just like ours. In our personal 
experience, they work well, and we deeply resonate with some of their principles, if not all. However, 
we designed our own non-exhaustive list of principles: 
 
Listen to your emotions – Pay attention to and accept your feelings  
and emotions: follow your gut feeling. 
 
Be open about how you feel – love your feeling and express how you  
feel to others when they should know about it. 
 
Be present – be part of your own life and others life, we are all here  
to have a good time. 
 
Stay critical – Always check your sources. 
 
One for all, all for one – people come first before anything else. 
 
Creative self-expression – we want you to actively engage in creating  
new manifestations of your self-expression, do whatever you want to  
do or express until you do not overshadow others’ experience. 
 
Radical collaboration –Help others, and don’t be afraid to reach out  
for help. 
 
Face your fears – challenging yourself leads to continued growth. 
 
Balance – it is all about balance in every field of life; strive to find 
 yours no matter where you are. 
 
Focus on your flow – it is the optimal state of the mind, be mindful 
 about you and others experience. 
 
==PAGE:95==
 
95 
 
5.3.2 Application procedure  
The analogy we used to design this very first interaction with Heterotopia was a bureaucratic 
procedure. This process is described by us with the following qualities: tedious, back and forth, 
unauthorized. In this sense, we want to make the application tedious and unappealing for one who is 
not deeply interested in becoming a member of Heterotopia. The following steps are detailing the 
procedure which has three distinguished parts. The application form can be seen on the next page. 
Part 1: Application Form. 
Everyone who wishes to join and become a co-owner of Heterotopia must fill out the application form. 
One has to fill out personal details and state his/her/their motivation to join. Because knowledge is in 
the center it is asked what one wants to teach, what one wants to learn from others. Lastly, we ask 
what one’s core moral values are. One must accept the principles of Heterotopia to become a member.  
Part 2: Making a Profile Picture. 
Like ID cards, applicants also need a picture representing them in the system. Usually, these pictures 
are boring. We wanted to make the process and the pictures more personal, playful, and self-
expressive. We added interactive and creative elements and reimagined the process. We turned the 
usually boring pictures into a crazy photoshoot where everyone can dress up in colorful clothes. For 
example, they can wear renaissance style clothes to resemble the portraits from this period. Some 
costumes will be provided by Heterotopia, but applicants are encouraged to bring their own style. The 
whole process is more like a photoshoot in a creative and crazy atmosphere than just making an ID 
picture.  
Part 3: Getting the final approval and submitting the Application. 
To finalize official documents, people are usually sent back and forth between institutes in the 
bureaucratic systems. Inspired by this mechanism we require applicants to get a stamp to finalize their 
application process. However, this stamp is given out by one of the members of Heterotopia. The 
applicants have to find this person and collect the stamp from them. The stamp can be collected inside 
Heterotopia during weekdays between 8-11 am and 4-7 pm. Then the application can be submitted in 
an envelope in the Application Box next to the entrance.  
The idea with is to make the applicants interact while they are trying to collect the stamp. Either they 
interact with other applicants or they ask help from people they see in Heterotopia. Maybe they ask 
for direction or they want to know who is giving out the stamps for them. We believe they will interact 
and engage with others while they are doing this. This will create their first experience with the place 
and hopefully a positive one.  
The idea in is to give applicants a minor task – in this case to find someone. We believe that this is the 
initial interaction requiring the biggest energy input from a person. Because of this we wanted to give 
them and excuse to talk to people. Therefore, when they are looking for the stamps, they have an 
excuse to approach others. This interaction will reduce the friction (concerns) which one might feel 
and make the first interaction easier.  
The application is the first step towards becoming a Heterotopian. Making the application somewhat 
difficult yet gamified and fun improves the user experience. Hopefully, it also filters the participants 
who are not a match with the concept – because they are not dedicated to do it.  
==PAGE:96==
 
96
