events industry. 
 
 
Artist 
 
Musician and leader of a 
small NGO 
 
Producer, Curator, and 
Performer 
 
Artist, Businessman, Coach, 
Production Manager 
 
 
Results 
Below are summarized the key points, sorted by the desired effects (numbered from 1 to 6) that we 
asked about. “BG” refers to Behavioral Goal and “SI” to the Social Implication. For each evaluated 
aspect, we describe what was decided to be further implemented. 
 
Increased social capital 
- 
(1BG) With its structured and diverse environment, meeting people where they are, 
Heterotopia will likely make people socialize more in person.  
- 
(1SI) If integration is successful and conflicts are handled correctly, Heterotopia will likely 
deepen and solidify its users’ social connections. 
- 
(2BG) Making people speak more openly about their feelings is challenging in new social 
structures, however Heterotopia will likely initiate this process because of its non-judgmental 
culture. 
- 
(2SI) Overall mental health & wellbeing is increased by an efficient tackling of social isolation. 
 
The social aspect of Heterotopia has been widely acknowledged by experts. This forms a good basis for 
the project, as it was our main aspect. As integration seemed to be crucial for this aspect, we detailed 
even further the welcoming ceremony, adding elements such as a rating procedure the buddies.  
 
More meaningful occupations 
- 
(3BG) Heterotopia will likely stimulate users to engage in activities they like, however it will 
require many resources to respond to everyone’s interests. 
- 
 (3SI) By encouraging experimentation, and by offering a “master-apprentice” mentorship 
system, Heterotopia will increase the population’s fulfillment and skills. 
- 
(4BG) Heterotopia will probably initiate collaborations between fields, but probably not sustain 
them. 
- 
(4SI) It is hard to say if Heterotopia’s endeavors will profit the greater good or stay within its 
own community. 
==PAGE:130==
 
130 
 
 
Providing the framework for people having more meaningful occupations was one of Heterotopia’s 
secondary goals. The desired behavioral goals and social implications were acknowledged by the vast 
majority of experts, praising the “master-apprentice” mentorship system and the diversity of activities 
offered. Such a diversity of activities will require a great diversity of people – some additional expert 
feedback pointed out that the current application procedure could result in a too homogenous group 
of members. However, the slow growth was judged effective – we decided to keep the current 
application framework but open up for ways to attract a larger variety of people. 
 
A more conscious population with a greater connection to nature 
- 
(5BG) A more direct relationship is needed to better strengthen Heterotopian’s connection to 
nature. 
- 
(5SI) Mixed opinions on whether Heterotopia will make its users take environmental concerns 
into consideration in work and private situations, more information needed.  
 
The frequent exposure to nature that Heterotopia provides to its users was acknowledged to be a very 
good starting point in the reconnection with nature. Most experts saw the potential for reconnecting 
people with nature, but that more elements were needed to make it effective. Several experts 
suggested that actively engaging with nature (such as gardening, being in real nature) would be needed 
to achieve the desired effect and potentially have effects outside Heterotopia.  This can be integrated 
in the workshops and activities happening at Heterotopia, by encouraging such activities.  
 
Increased presence 
- 
(6BG) People’s presence is likely to be increased, but more is needed to insure a state of 
immediacy. 
- 
(6SI) More information about the design proposal was needed to assess that Heterotopia will 
increase one's general productivity and deepens one's social relations. 
 
Almost all experts find it believable that users will be very present inside of Heterotopia. One expert 
even recognized the 4 conditions to achieve the state of flow, as enunciated by Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi 
(1990) (who first defined this so-called “optimal” state of mind) in Heterotopia. However, the concept 
needs more to boost its user’s productivity, and this could be further developed on. 
 
Below the answers are summarized into a paragraph for each question asked. They are sorted by the 
desired effects (numbered from 1 to 6) that we asked about. They are marked as “BG” for Behavioral 
Goal and “SI” for Social Implication. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:131==
 
131 
 
Increased social capital 
(1BG) According to the feedback, all 13 experts believe that Heterotopia will make its users spend more 
time socializing in person. By bringing in likeminded people, cultivating a culture of acceptance and 
inclusion of its members, it creates a psychologically safe environment fostering growth in a social 
context. Furthermore, Heterotopia appears to offer a structured environment that provides 
opportunities to meet at different levels and in different ways. At the same time, it also considers that 
a lot of people have also an introverted nature at times and might desire a calmer environment. This 
makes people feel included and creates a sense of belonging and community, creating the desire to do 
it frequently. 
(1SI) Most experts (12/13) agreed that its believable that Heterotopia will contribute to people 
acquiring and maintaining more and stronger social connections, relying on the success of the first 
steps and first impressions of the first new members. By providing a place for people who are feeling 
isolated to connect but also have ways to contribute and take inspiration to apply to their own lives, 
they will feel very welcome. Sharing a similar mindset or interest in a same skill is very connecting and 
fulfilling and can ignite further collaborations. However, there was a concern that collective dimension 
of the community can also create hostility because. As people are people, not machines, and it may be 
difficult to invent a social setting that will work out perfectly well for everybody, however ideal the 
intentions. 
(2BG) A non-judgmental culture gives individuals a sense of security that their viewpoints will not 
discredit them. In a community like this, set up to enable trust, confidence, and encouragement among 
the members, most experts found it believable that Heterotopia will stimulate its users to speak more 
openly about their values and emotions. However, two experts noted their concern, one of them 
stating that “Social structures that substitute both family and work are hard to build and tend to slide 
into sect-like groups.”. The other highlights the importance and difficulty of creating the right culture 
from the very beginning, and how it’s very difficult to create once established. 
(2SI) All experts supported the belief that it is believable that Heterotopia will contribute to increased 
mental health and wellbeing. Social isolation is real and detrimental to humans and it is especially 
important for people who live alone in small apartments to get out of and socialize. Heterotopia offers 
people an opportunity to socialize in person and get physical exercise outside their apartments, which 
will improve mental health and well-being. If one feels secure to be themselves, the internal emotional, 
physical, and spiritual issues they face can more easily be openly discussed. This will allow more 
opportunities for others to provide viable and applicable support in resolving these issues, whether 
through advice or action. 
More meaningful occupations 
(3BG) Most experts found it (11/13) believable that Heterotopia will stimulate its users to engage in 
activities and projects they like. Naturally, if people have the desire to follow certain pursuits they will 
if the opportunity is there. The situation described in Heterotopia seems to be encouraging people in 
this, and especially when there are tools and mentors around. However, to respond to everybody's 
interests - it must be a huge place. The other experts needed more information to agree. 
(3SI) All but one (who required more information) found it believable that Heterotopia will contribute 
to a more fulfilled and skilled population. Spaces like Heterotopia encourage experimentation, which 
naturally leads to acquiring new skills, as people continue to pursue certain "experiments" and new 
activities over time and become better at them from a place of choice. That the mentor-student 
==PAGE:132==
 
132 
 
learning system sounds like the best way to master a skill. First you need a student who is interested 
(by his or her own motivation) and then secondly a mentor who teaches from a daily experience. The 
process is fulfilling for both - for the student of learning new things and for the mentor - having 
someone really interested in the craft and showing/asking about new ways of doing the craft. 
(4BG) 10 out of 13 found it believable that Heterotopia will make its users initiate and sustain 
collaborations (professional or amateur) across different fields. Here we selected the most extensive 
feedback, which also reflected parts of others feedback:  
“If Heterotopia is expanding to other cities it would imply duration of existing "branches" and 
local relationships. Making the library of best practices available online will encourage proliferation of 
the concepts and similar spaces opening, but in your model someone in each location needs to assume 
responsibility for the physical spaces that are rented or purchased. That will mean there has to be solid 
leadership or a sustainable leadership process (and process of transition) in each location too interface 
with the city and any regulating agencies. With ownership of property there must be financing/income 
and I didn't see mention of that--although I can assume there are something like membership fees, 
pooling resources, or financial benefactors. This is where sometimes it is better to have a social group 
that is not anchored by physical venues and can host gatherings in free spaces throughout cities. That 
is how, for example, The Cacophony Society in the United States had success. They were inclusive in 
their "membership", did not require any payments (although you could subscribe to a newsletter) and 
they were not tied down by property ownership or overhead. They held their events in public spaces 
and each other's apartments, and in unused and forgotten public spaces. But there are also successful 
art spaces throughout the world based upon the Principles of Burning Man, for example, that are 
running well enough. A constant challenge is how to manage changes in leadership over time. Setting 
up non-profits with clear bylaws and operating procedures with codes of conduct have helped, but 
whenever there is property involved it creates a layer of financial complexity that can be difficult to 
sustain over time. So, I do wonder if Heterotopia will be able to continue to scale and endure. I watched 
a maker space based on membership fees that became a chain collapse after a few years.” The 3 who 
answered no required more information. 
(4SI) There was a mixed response if the experts found it believable that Heterotopia will stimulate 
people to create and/or participate in meaningful social ventures beneficial for the greater good. Even 
though all experts answered yes, half of them needed further information to be fully convinced. It is 
difficult to say whether Heterotopia’s endeavors will profit the greater good or merely their own small 
community. But some of the social ventures will probably profit the common good. Communities of 
many kinds that are built around the value of doing good for others and a higher purpose will have the 
potential to bring about that affect, including Heterotopia. That is, if members adhere to that value 
and explicitly push and expect other members to do the same and act accordingly. 
 
A more conscious population with a greater connection to nature 
(5BG) It was a mixed response if it was believable that Heterotopia will increase its user's individual 
connection to nature and sense of environmental responsibility. Most experts needed more 
information to be convinced but found it possible to achieve. The following illustrates their combined 
feedback quite well: 
“It would be more believable if there were shared experiences in nature, instead of just indoors. 
To care for nature, one must have direct experience and a relationship with it. Do people at Heterotopia 
have to care for the gardens themselves? If so, that would build a stronger relationship. Do they care 
==PAGE:133==
 
133 
 
for animals and form relationships with them? Do they have outings in nature with positive 
experiences? I think a basis for caring for nature could be strengthened in your narrative. Think about 
how people form relationships with nature that is not just a manicured version of it indoors.” 
(5SI) 50% found it possible that Heterotopia will make people take environmental concerns into 
consideration in work and private situations but needed more info to convinced. Environmentally 
aware talks were said to potentially create positive social pressure to take environmental concerns 
into consideration in social settings. The sense of community and introduction of information about 
the environment described would hopefully influence people to take these issues more seriously and 
consider them at home or work. Some advice to support our intended behavior was by making it fun 
to do environmentally positive behaviors. On the other hand, there was issued concerns of elements 
that could support self-absorbedness that could create the opposite of environmental concerns.  
 
Increased presence 
(6BG) All but three (who needed more information) found it believable that Heterotopia can make its 
users more present while they are engaged in an activity. By having an experience that is personalized 
and welcoming in a familiar community where people can explore and enjoy activities together, it 
seems very likely it will make people more present, even more so if they are motivated to learn or 
mentor about things that matter to them. One expert stated:  
“Flow State is the culmination of heightened focus on the task at hand. Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi’s 
external conditions are:  
1. Novel Experience - Something new is happening. In a diverse environment of many creative 
people, this will likely be a consistent factor.  
2. Rich environment - There is a lot of stimulating elements in the environment. Which is likely in 
such a hub as Heterotopia.  
3. Deep Embodiment - As many of the senses are being utilized or immersed. Again, likely in a 
creative hub” 
“One can assume members will be more present than experiencing things online, but that is not the 
same as being truly present and experiencing a state of immediacy. If you want to convey that, it must 
be more explicit in the value system. It is not unbelievable that Heterotopia makes people more present 
while they are engaged in an activity, but from this narrative it’s not that specifically addressed. 
However, I do expect people to be naturally more present if they are in the company of others.” 
(6SI) Mixed reactions where 50% desired more information to be convinced to find it believable that 
Heterotopia will increase one's general productivity and deepen one's social relations. One questioned 
if it would work out that way for everybody. However, those who were convinced, stated that by 
providing opportunities to socialize and practice social skills, and allowing people to learn and mentor, 
it seems likely that it leads to a deepening of one's social relations. One reason being it requires 
listening and taking turns. Because of the co-creating likeminded relations in a positive psychological 
safe environment, it can create an open-mindedness to create, learn, live and love. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:134==
 
134 
 
 
Chapter 7:  
Reflection 
 
 
 
==PAGE:135==
 
135 
 
This Reflection aims to identify, analyze, and reflect on what we learned during this report's 
development. First, we are going to reflect on the learning objectives of this thesis. Second, we will 
critically analyze and reflect on the challenges of the methods we used to develop the project. Finally, 
we will reflect on what we did during the project to fulfill our learning objectives and the process itself. 
Reflection on the general Learning Objectives of DTU: 
The project took the general learning objectives from DTU as the basis of this thesis. A graduate of the 
MSc program from DTU: 
• 
Can identify and reflect on technical, scientific issues and understand the interaction between 
the various components that make up an issue:  
 
During the development of this thesis, we analyzed a complex socio-cultural problem using the 
ViP method. Using this visionary design tool, we reframed the entertainment industry's future 
in the domain of: “Entertainment with socializing purposes in the city”. From this, we identified 
a need to feel more collectively connected (statement) in the future, which eventually led us 
to develop an appropriate solution in the form of a visionary social space called Heterotopia. 
 
• 
Can, on the basis of a clear academic profile, apply elements of current research at 
international level to develop ideas and solve problems. 
• 
Is familiar with and can seek out leading international research within his/her/their specialist 
area. 
 
The final concept – Heterotopia – was developed with extensive research in mental health and 
wellbeing, social sustainability, third places, and more (see Chapter 3.1: Literature review). 
This research was, together with the interview of experts, utilized to develop our final design. 
We interviewed 16 experts who are working within our domain, and they gave us invaluable 
guidance and knowledge on what contributed to the development of this project. Many of 
them closely followed up on the project's development, as they found it inspiring and exciting. 
 
• 
Masters technical scientific methodologies, theories and tools, and has the capacity to take a 
holistic view of and delimit a complex, open issue, see it in a broader academic and societal 
perspective and, on this basis, propose a variety of possible actions. 
 
Used the ViP and SID method, combining the two approaches, fulfilling each other's 
imperfections. We mostly followed the ViP method's steps and took the necessary inspiration 
from the SID method - as we were working with social implication design. Utilizing different 
aspects of the two methods allowed us to have a more refined approach to the way we were 
gathering, analyzing and utilizing data. In turn, this helped us to create a concept that is heavily 
grounded in reality. 
 
We continuously reflected on our process and presented our project to our close friends, who 
gave invaluable feedback and motivation. Near the end of the project, we recorded a video 
interview with our colleagues to discuss the project's challenges, which we will analyze once 
the project is delivered.  
 
As we implemented various methods and theories in working with ViP and SID, we could get 
better results for our project. We used the Business Model Canvas, Unique Value Proposition 
==PAGE:136==
 
136 
 
Canvas, Competitive Benchmarking, and the Blue Ocean strategy in the Concepting phase to 
draft a business model and compare our solution's innovativeness. We used elements of 
Conceptual Design (Andreasen et al., 2015) to detail out parts of the final solution. Visualizing 
our thoughts through drawings was frequently used to develop the concept to make it easier 
to understand each other and the idea, which proved to be a very efficient communication 
tool.  
 
• 
Can, via analysis and modelling, develop relevant models, systems and processes for solving 
technological problems. 
First, we analyzed almost 500 qualitative context factors during the development of the 
solution. We organized them into clusters and made assumptions of them regarding the future 
of our domain. We then imagined a world of the likely future anchored in hard research and 
scientific evidence upon these assumptions. This attempt at clairvoyance was used to design a 
more desirable future, eventually leading up to the vision statement as a reaction to the likely 
future. 
Secondly, we used the SID method to identify six desired behavior goals connected to our 
statement. To fulfill the goal of the statement, we had to make strong mechanisms. These 
mechanisms are means or solutions that help to reach the behavior goals and the final 
statement. To design these mechanisms, we analyzed people and identified their concerns and 
their clashes with society. After selecting the relevant concerns, we developed the 
mechanisms to solve them, developed in ideation sessions (like brainstorming) where we 
iterated on the concept in multiple stages and scales.  
The reason for designing in increments was that when we could see that the manifestation of 
the concept of Heterotopia was taking form as a physical space, the importance of considering 
growth became evident. A physical concept can only last for as long as it’s designed for, 
consciously or implicitly. We mean and argue that being conscious about how a place will grow 
and how its participants and members will react is crucial from the very beginning. The reason 
being, to take both sustainable and socially sustainable behaviors, mechanisms, and economy 
into consideration. This being the same reason we argue for the immense value of increased 
transparency. Suppose a place’s supporters can see from the very beginning by using a holistic 
approach, a clear intention, and a vision of a place made to scale. As long as the transparently 
stays consistent, it will be logical why an initial supporter should support and continue to do 
so.  
• 
Can communicate and mediate research-based knowledge both orally and in writing 
This report's documentation was challenging because the ViP method does not have any 
guidance on documenting the process. It is a rather creative approach where one can quickly 
generate a lot of data and move on without documenting. A reflection from the process we 
now recognize is how hard it can be to follow up on all this information, both from an outsider's 
view and even for the designers themselves. We generated a large amount of data through 
research, interviews, and ideation sessions. The process's outcome is presented in this report 
in a logical order as we followed the process's steps.  
• 
Can work independently and reflect on own learning, academic development, and 
specialization 
==PAGE:137==
 
137 
 
The Covid-19 pandemic and the following restrictions deeply affected our design process. We 
were mostly working in our closed social circle where we created a bubble – sort of a focused 
mindset around this report's domain. We regularly presented and explained our design process 
and the project to others who gave us feedback and suggestions. Further reflection regarding 
our process and the method itself can be found below this section.  
• 
Masters technical problem-solving at a high level through project work, and has the capacity 
to work with and manage all phases of a project – including preparation of timetables, design, 
solution and documentation 
Being three designers on an open and highly abstract topic required to take positions and 
differentiate team roles. We mapped out the strength and weaknesses of each group 
member. Based on our characteristics, we differentiated the following team roles – project 
manager, process manager, and documentation manager. To eradicate the possibility of 
internal conflict, we turned the roles into personas. These personas were then introduced to 
the team and used as roleplaying exercises. We used these personas to act like different 
stakeholders, creating motivation, authority, and a flat hierarchy in the project.  
However, working during the Covid-19 pandemic was not easy. We were mentally and 
physically restricted in acting freely, resulting in difficulties maintaining a healthy motivation 
and reaching out to stakeholders and people of interest.  
 
Reflection on using the ViP method 
In this section, we will reflect on the main method we used during the development of this master’s 
thesis. We are going to reflect on the design process and give a critical analysis of it. We are going to 
analyze what was good and what could have been better. Then we will identify the main reasons 
behind these issues and give critical feedback on them and suggestions on how to improve.  
 
What we found good in working with ViP: 
• 
The ViP method allowed us to use scientific research and combine it with our opinions to form 
a new visionary and innovative concept. The final concept synthesizes the literature review's 
knowledge and our gut feeling to take a position and address the desired future scenario (See 
future context in Chapter 4, Structuring the concept). The ViP method argues that the 
designers should follow his/her/their intuitions because the design process can never be truly 
objective and value-free (Hekkert & Van Dijk, 2011). The method gives a lot of power and 
freedom to the designer to challenge the current status-quo. Therefore, allows him/her/them 
to carry out an innovative design synthesis. We deeply resonate with this mindset, and we 
believe designers should always feel free to follow their intuitions to make decisions. We all 
gained a lot from using this innovative method. It broadened our horizons, made us more 
confident in decision making, and more experienced in working in the field of visionary design.  
 
• 
Because ViP allows intuitions to guide us, our approach to the design process was dual. We 
used logical arguments and scientific evidence to reason our intuitions and emotions. The 
combination of logic and emotions allowed the concept to grow freely in an open-minded 
environment where scientific evidence supported steady growth, resulting in a visionary 
design solution. We used this duality in the form of a narrative story to describe the concept's 
user experience. In this story, we painted the emotions to reflect what emotions we feel 
==PAGE:138==
 
138 
 
towards the concept. Furthermore, we added callouts to explain the logic behind it. Being met 
with positive reactions from our surroundings for our concept and drawings, these were 
continuously developed and improved. 
What was difficult in working with ViP: 
• 
The most difficult part of the process was communication. First, we will reflect on the internal 
communication part within the group – between the three members. Even though we spoke 
the same language, we did not understand each other all the time. It was challenging to work 
on an abstract social phenomenon, mostly because we are not native English speakers. 
Because of this, we had to make sure that we had the same idea in the head as we were 
developing the concept. This was inefficient and tiresome as we had to do it frequently. When 
we had to make decisions, which pivoted the concept towards another direction, we had to 
make sure everyone knew everything he needs to know. Thus, we built the concept using some 
words that we invented and agreed on its meaning–like collectively connected. Using 
dictionaries to understand what we mean by our words was an essential part of developing 
our internal language.  
 
• 
Second, external communication with stakeholders of the project. External communication 
throughout the whole project was complex and difficult. In the ViP-method, the designer starts 
on a very abstract level, gradually getting closer to the more concrete level toward the end. 
This makes the project hard to explain to outsiders who are not familiar with the method, as 
neither the designer does not know themselves what the to-be-designed product is before the 
end of the project. This means involving a stakeholder during the development process is 
complicated and requires a lot of trust. Even for people familiar with the project, it was hard 
to understand what we were doing at any given time. The great thing about this method is 
how much knowledge you gain in your domain and the security that the final concept rests on 
this foundation. However, this is a double-edged sword since the designers are in a bubble, 
and it is hard to communicate the process outwards. One of the reasons this is hard is that 
there are no documentation tools in the ViP method. Thus, it was challenging to create 
deliverables for the stakeholders and follow up on the project's development.   
Reflection on the project: 
In this section, we are reflecting on the project and the teamwork. We analyze what was good and 
what could have been better. We are trying to identify the main reasons behind these issues and give 
critical feedback on them and suggestions on how to improve.  
What was good about working with the project: 
• 
The most valuable part of the project was to work on something we were also passionate 
about. We created the project to gain invaluable knowledge in the domain and use this insight 
in our future careers. We had numerous experiences and a very strong network in the 
entertainment industry domain, so it seemed like the logical thing to do. We created the 
concept of Heterotopia on solid foundations in research and science while we designed the 
place to support our current and future generation’s needs. We received a lot of feedback and 
support from our environment, who offered their help to further develop the concept after 
the delivery of this paper. This genuine support and acknowledgment show to us the 
appropriateness of our design as well as the need we tackled in this report. What we gain from 
the project further is recognition in our network by developing a visionary concept. 
==PAGE:139==
 
139 
 
 
• 
Secondly, we are ultimately grateful that we were introduced to the ViP method by our 
supervisor. ViP completely reframed our design approach and changed the way we see 
product design today. This was, without question, one of the most important and enjoyable 
parts of the process.  
 
What could have been better: 
• 
Our time management was the topic we received most criticism on. The following reasons 
contributed to this: first, we have not had any hierarchy or roles differentiating the team 
members at the beginning of the project. As our scope was abstract and the research exciting 
and widespread, we diverged for too long and created a large amount of data we had to 
analyze through an active discussion about the topics verbally. As no one was controlling this 
process, we used a lot of time on it. As we saw the need for management within the group, 
we differentiated team roles based on our strengths and weaknesses. We divided areas of 
responsibility which made us work faster and more efficiently. To maintain the excellent flow 
and transparent communication, we made the team roles into personas – representing 
stakeholders – so everyone acted according to their interests. We did this to avoid internal 
conflict and differentiate our friendship from the working environment. Eventually, the 
personas turned into well-functioning and frequently used methods of development. We 
pivoted them and changed our roles from time-to-time. It was a very good learning objective 
to observe and improve the group dynamic with the personas. It is very helpful in conflict 
management since the persona can be differentiated from us. Even though we were able to 
reflect on our time management and improve it, we had to extend the deadline of this project. 
This was somewhat because of Covid-19, which directly affected one of the group members 
and us in general. 
 
• 
Secondly the ViP method was less familiar to us, so we spent a lot of time trying to find the 
right way to use it. It was more difficult than using the other well-known design methods – like 
blue ocean or, business model canvas. However, it was an invaluable learning experience. 
Using ViP felt like standing on the top of a mountain, looking down and having a great overview 
on these other methods. From this imaginary mountain we – the designers – could select the 
right methods, like puzzle pieces and successfully integrate them to the ViP process. The ViP 
method allowed us to see other methods – maybe our whole designer degree – from a higher, 
abstract design perspective. 
 
To summarize the reflection:  
We gained a lot of knowledge using ViP. Most importantly, to see and identify needs and develop 
visionary solutions for them. Secondly, to work with abstract topics and stretch the boundaries of 
what is possible in a design engineering context. We learned how to reflect in action on our process 
and improve it immediately.  
Doing this project gave us a lot of excitement and joy. We worked passionately every day while our 
vision guided us. Only at the end – now – when we started to share what we made, we received so 
much positive feedback on it. Many of the people who contributed to the project showed their 
interest in making it happen. We believe this is the most significant and crucial validation that our 
visionary project – Heterotopia – could receive.  
==PAGE:140==
 
140 
 
 
 
Chapter 8:  
Discussion & Conclusion 
 
 
==PAGE:141==
 
141 
 
8.1 Discussion 
This chapter will discuss the result of the project, evaluating our concept against our vision. 
Furthermore, we discuss current limitations, and future work.  
The result 
Working within the domain of “Entertainment with socializing purposes in the city”, the goal of our 
project statement was to make “people to feel collectively connected while they are engaged in an 
event”. According to our desired future, our research pointed to six interaction qualities to fulfill the 
statement: solemn, physical, reliable, encouraging, playful, and uncomfortable. Turning these 
interaction qualities to product qualities like: Ritualized, Meaningful, Atmospheric, Supportive, 
Genuine and Creative, eventually being used to develop our final concept, a world within worlds; 
Heterotopia.  
Evaluating our concept against our vision: 
- “We want people to feel collectively connected while they are engaged in an event.” 
As the result being a highly interactive and creative space, designed to be supportive and motivate any 
individual in self-actualization through creativity, authentic self-expression. We would argue that this 
leads to being more Connected with oneself. By sharing this space with likeminded people (ensured 
by our application procedure), supported by the buddy and mentoring system, we believe it will lead 
to familiarity and inclusion. When those who choose to apply can see the purpose of the place, 
supporting each other and partaking in similar ventures towards self-actualization, we would argue it 
leads to feeling similar and Connected with others.  
We hope our concept appeals to a wide diversity of members, both in ethnicity, culture, nationality, 
and sexuality. In this designed safe space, we hope to expand people’s open-mindedness, leading to a 
common understanding, seeing the universal similarities in all humans. We argue that understanding 
the world and how we are all as individuals’ part of one big complex ecosystem is essential to feel 
Connected to the globalized world. As Heterotopia is a concept designed to grow and expand, we 
hope that this open-mindedness and encouragement towards collective self-actualization can reach 
the furthest corners of the planet, one way or the other. 
Implementation and Future work 
To implement this successfully, we are aware of that there is still a long way to go. We recognize that 
our concept's future requires further investigation, research, iteration, testing, together with further 
validation from experts in the topics such as economy, psychology, and sociology. As one of our experts 
highlighted: “One of the challenges of the model that you propose is how to deal with 
liability/ownership /management/financial issues that spaces like you propose typically involve.” We 
recognize as having been only 3 people designing, that our concept's future requires further 
investigation, research, iteration, and testing. Together with further validation from experts in the 
topics such as economy, psychology, and sociology we hope to overcome potential aeras for conflict 
such as ideological and cultural differences. 
 
==PAGE:142==
 
142 
 
8.2 Conclusion 
 
The aim of this project was to investigate the trends and developments shaping the entertainment 
industry ten years in the future (2031). Analyzing the individual and society's desires and needs at 
large, we worked towards designing a better and more meaningful way to create community and 
gather in cities.  
This project results from a process of in-depth research in various seemingly different fields, aiming to 
develop a coherent picture of the future. This knowledge was utilized in synergy with interviews from 
many of the world’s most excellent event organizers, kind enough to share their expertise and support 
us throughout our Herculean journey. 
So far, this process resulted in developing a strong vision and principles that became the drivers behind 
the development of this visionary project. Guided by our vision and motivation, we designed a mindset 
with a set of core moral principles that serve as a basis for urbanites to create valuable, scalable, and 
sustainable urban communities. Using these principles, we detailed and refined a jaw-dropping 
environment with various experiences in a highly intertwined space. This space does not only function 
as a gathering place for its community members but aims to become a meaningful part of their lives 
and support them in their self-actualization pursuits.  
The key focus was on creating a new meaningful third place for urbanites to gather, emphasizing the 
importance of nature, celebration, music, self-expression, and community. Our research shows that 
by bringing this more into people’s daily life, through mediums like rituals, it holds power to both 
maintain and improve "a healthy mind in a healthy body“( From the Latin expression “Mens sana in 
corpore sano” (“Mens Sana in Corpore Sano,” n.d.)).  
Being in a diverse environment of like-minded people where all are encouraged to actively engage in 
new relationships, enables the members to broaden their horizons, foster new collaborations, and 
challenge their values. However, after feedback we recognized the need to actively avoid echo 
chambers within the concept. The importance is not just being transparent, but also to actively 
encourage members in staying knowledgeable, critical, and well informed as a world citizen.  
With our concept we support creativity, artistry, and the pursuit of every human to become the best 
version of themselves, while encouraging them in giving back to their community and humanity.

==PAGE:155==

Appendix 
 
A: Preparation: ‘Deconstructing’ full: 
A.1 16+ club 
 
Product Level 
The venue is a club with a dancefloor with seating hubs along it incrusted in the wall, and a slightly 
elevated area by the bar.  The walls are black as well as the columns circling the small stairs between 
the bar area and the dancefloor. Because of the flash it is hard to judge the lighting - but it doesn’t 
seem to be the most impressive. The chandeliers by the sitting hubs and the dimmed red lights give a 
fancy touch, in strong contrast with the black walls, the sweaty atmosphere and the very young-looking 
guests filling the space. The surroundings look like an originally fancy place converted into a less fancy 
club. The elevation of the bar area makes the dancefloor look like a amphitheater scene. 
 
Interaction Level 
The venue is packed with young people, composed of more boys than girls. A lot of different 
interactions are happening at the same time, in a disorganized way: some are dancing together, some 
are trying to get through the dancefloor, a group of people seem to talk together by the bar… People 
seem to be grouped in relatively closed groups of people, up against each other yet not interacting 
directly. What is striking is that there are eyes pointing in all directions. Some are looking at other 
people (mostly people that don’t seem to be part of their group), some nowhere (disengaged looks). 
==PAGE:156==
 
156 
 
There is an overall feeling of confusion, counterbalanced by some people dancing people seeming to 
enjoy themselves. There is nothing people are united around, no front, no back.  While the mass of 
people on the dancefloor is completely disorganized, there is slightly more order by the bar, where 
people are waiting for their drinks. But even there everyone is pointing in a different direction and look 
unsettled. 
Context Level 
The concept could evolve around making young people feel empowered. This venue provides an early 
ticket to the clubbing universe (allows 16 y/o instead of 18 y/o). This premature access plays with the 
curiosity and the desire to look/act older, popular for this age group. The try-to-look-fancy look 
emphasizes the empowerment that can be felt by the young clubbers. The concept appeals to some 
of the brains basic drives - social status (the fancy-like surroundings, the altered state of mind after 
consumption of alcohol), and desire for sex - that are felt stronger in younger brains (Bohler, 2019). 
Other similar events are advertised targeting the same drives. 
 
The latter is also relevant for regular clubs (for older people). In this example it is just even more 
obvious because it  
 
 
==PAGE:157==
 
157 
 
A.2 Masada 
 
Product level:  
The product is many things at once in this case. It 
is every object that belongs to the interior of the 
club. There is an inside and outside area. The inside 
is the dancefloor, and the outside is a calm-down 
chill area. The interior inside is very cozy, 
compared to regular clubs. First of all, the room is 
dimly lit with yellow lights in every corner and it 
illuminates the whole environment creating a 
comfortable and bright interior. Behind the DJ desk 
there is a big bookshelf structure and in front of it 
are regular objects that can be found in every living 
room. This design with the occasional appearance 
of random couches and sofas creates a living-room 
vibe. The outside area is located under a roof which 
provides shelter from rain. There are many chairs 
and seating opportunities also a few precious sofas 
can be found in the back of this area. Everything in the interior looks old and vintage, grandma style 
living room and other places where old and unwanted furniture’s can be found. The whole 
environment looks friendly and welcoming. There are pictures hanging on the wall. Opposite to the 
dancefloor there is a bar and a space with tables and chairs. The people are also part of the 
environment because the crowd influences each other, and it creates an echo or reflection in 
everyone. People are wearing whatever they want to. You can find all kind of self-expression. What’s 
is still common in the crowd is the fact that they all feel original and authentic in the clothes that they 
are wearing. It shines their personalities or their moods.  
 
Interaction level: 
As you enter the club you feel you are in a familiar place. This feeling of familiarity allows you to spend 
less energy on evaluating the possible dangers this place might hide. And you can focus on discovering 
how you want to interact with the space. I would describe this interaction as playfulness. Curiosity that 
makes people wonder and trying out different things than they usually do. This can involve dressing 
for self-expression, interacting with the interior on a different level, talking to guests and other people 
in the club as genuine interest. So, the interaction is genuine and authentic too. Another interaction 
quality is something I would call unconstrained. Since everyone is moving around freely as they please. 
No-one is pretending but rather expressing what they are feeling or want to do. These interactions are 
promoted by the feeling of safe environment and the shared feeling of belonging to this place. Since 
the club requires membership card to get it and it is highly underground only the ones who belong to 
a specific group/ social circle/ have the same interests can find it. This makes the crowd united into 
one family. Another interaction is something with the perception of the place and the time what 
people experience inside. It is a retreat or getaway. It is sacred in a way. 
 
 
==PAGE:158==
 
158 
 
Context level: 
The designer of this place can be the owner or the interior designer or some other person who worked 
on the vision. It is hard to tell who did what but the end result is a seamlessly working product. First of 
all, it is important to mention that the club is located in the residential area of Milan between flats in 
the middle of this concrete jungle. When you walk in through the gate you also disappear in time and 
space and leave the busy ugly city behind. This works magickly well in Masada even better than other 
clubs since the interior is a whole well-functioning environment. So, the thoughts behind the place 
might be to provide a getaway from the city and make a second home. The designer wanted a retreat 
for the guest of the place. It is furnished with vintage furniture to imitate a friendly familiar 
environment. The people who come here are always happy to talk with you because they feel like 
everyone is part of the same ‘family’.  
All in all the context is to help people gather and create an atmosphere with familiar faces every week 
where you can form friendships and make bonds over time while you are losing your sense of the 
outside world and thus focusing on the present only.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:159==
 
159 
 
A.3 Stavanger Cathedral 
 
Product level 
It’s an earnest and huge space that is mostly consisting of stone 
and elements of wood. The wooden floor is plain, while the 
stone columns that are holding up an integral part of the 
structure is quite dominating. It is a heavily decorated interior 
radiating importance, where spotlights are highlighting 
elements of focus and significance. Highly complex, static, and 
mobile elements are filling the room, where a wide selection of 
materials has been used, possibly with different intentions in mind, either practical or symbolic. There 
are massive, decorated windows illustrating different characters of great importance, made of mosaic 
in multicolored glass. The room is filled with individual wooden chairs meant for the audience. In the 
very back there is a wooden altar that has a serious and traditional tone over it. The element with the 
most ornamental carvings is the wooden pulpit. 
Interaction level 
       
 
The windows are illuminating the room by the sun shining through it from the outside, giving the room 
a unique atmosphere. The seating is giving each participant a sense of individuality in their 
togetherness. The pulpit is raised well above the surrounding floor for audibility and visibility. It is 
perhaps the element drawing the most attention as the priest is preaching to the audience from above. 
The altar is where the priest can perform rituals.  
Context 
The space is meant for gatherings and a variety of rituals. It does not 
seem to be equally participatory oriented as the pulpit and altar is 
more like a stage, like a theatre where something is performed in front 
of a silent audience. However, the dynamic changes when it goes from 
being a performance to a co-creational singsong and sing-along. The 
power dynamics is in general is where the priest is like a director or 
chief conductor in charge of carrying out the symphony, or a shepherd 
herding his flock in an egoless ceremony or performance.

Scientific Articles 
Cigna 2018 US Loneliness Index(Cigna, 2018) 
The survey was fielded among more than 20,000 U.S. adults ages 18 and older. According to the 
survey, many Americans experience feelings of loneliness, including:  
- 
When asked how often they feel like no one knows them well, more than half of the 
respondents (54%) surveyed said they feel that way always or sometimes. (Cigna, 2018) 
- 
Just under half of all those surveyed report sometimes or always feeling alone (46%) and/or 
feeling left out (47%). (Cigna, 2018) 
- 
At least two in five surveyed sometimes or always feel as though they lack companionship 
(43%), that their relationships are not meaningful (43%), that they are isolated from others 
(43%), and/or that they are no longer close to anyone (39%). (Cigna, 2018) 
- 
Approximately six in ten (59%) surveyed always/sometimes feel that their interests and 
ideas are not shared by those around them. (Cigna, 2018) 
- 
A similar proportion surveyed reports sometimes or always feeling like the people around 
them are not necessarily with them (56%). (Cigna, 2018) 
- 
Though fewer feel as though there is no one they can turn to, more than a third of the 
respondents nevertheless report feeling this way at least sometimes (36%). (Cigna, 2018) 
- 
Loneliness has the same impact on mortality as smoking 15 cigarettes a day, making it even 
more dangerous than obesity. (Cigna, 2018)

How to change your behavior for the better (Ariely, n.d.) 
- 
Giving information to people is just not a good recipe to change behavior. 
- 
If we want to change behavior, we have to change the environment. The right way is not to 
change people, it's to change the environment.  
- 
think about behavioral change in the same way that we think about sending a rocket to 
space. When we think about sending a rocket to space, we want to do two main things. The 
first one is to reduce friction. We want to take the rocket and have as little friction as 
possible so it's the most aerodynamic possible. And the second thing is we want to load as 
much fuel as possible, to give it the most amount of motivation, energy to do its task. And 
behavior change is the same thing. 
- 
If you reduce the price of something for, let's say, 10 cents to one cent, nothing much 
happens. You reduce it from one cent to zero, now people get excited. 
- 
This is the story of friction: small things really matter. And friction is about taking the desired 
behavior and saying: Where do we have too much friction so it's slowing people down from 
acting on it? And every time you see that the desired behavior and the easy behavior are 
not aligned, it means we want to try and realign them. 
- 
A little bit of guilt always works.

experience they're having with you in that moment and what they experience elsewhere. 
And they want it. It’s a magnet

Designing for society(Tromp & Hekkert, 2018) 
 
- 
“For integration to happen at all, people have to meet each other. They have to experience 
for themselves that their differences are not a barrier to humane interaction.” (p.3) 
- 
The facilitation of day-to-day, mundane interactions leads to an increase of familiarity and 
a decrease of tension. (p.3) 
- 
“Frequent gardeners interact scientifically more with their neighbors (Kullberg, 2016).” 
- 
“Digital technology and it’s services it enables have removed any banal reason we might

the corner which is open all night”. (p.4) 
-

"In designing our man-made world, we have designed our problems too. Designers have 
created the cars of peoples dreams, and now they have to design us right out of those cars, 
to save us from the environmental destruction they cause!" (p.3) 
- 
We designed an environment in which there is little need to build relationships with our 
neighbours, we have also decreased the likelihood of bumping into them accidentally. 
- 
Today's societies require design practices that prioritize societal values, rather than merely 
addressing economic, production or consumer demands. 
- 
Products and services are a means of making socially beneficial behaviors more meaningful 
to people. 
- 
The main focus for commercial design has been, and is, individual needs and desires. 
-

foster long-term happiness for users. 
- 
The interconnectivity of design and cultural factors can shape behavior over time. 
- 
Participatory design should be on the vibrant dialogues between groups with opposing 
matters of concern. 
- 
Designers should work towards resilient and sustainable systems that foster general long 
term well-being. 
- 
Designers should keep things small and stay local. 
- 
Working with local communities provides the freedom to develop radical alternatives that 
would run quickly ashore within the public sector. 
- 
Without a systemic approach, design interventions might solve issues locally but cause harm 
elsewhere. 
- 
A design whose objective is social - and thus seeks to improve society as a whole - requires 
designers to explicitly articulate what is of benefit to society as a whole and in the long run. 
It requires imagining a better future, rather than improved today

Websites 
Sunday Assembly (Sunday Assembly London, n.d.) 
- 
“Elementary Forms of Religious Life Durkheim famously stated that the ancient gods grow 
old or die, and others are not yet born^ (2001, 322). His suggestion was that although 
==PAGE:169==
 
169 
 
specific religious dogmas and other supernatural objects of belief evolve or depart, that 
something will always emerge in their place by virtue and dint of the dynamics of human 
group life itself. In one sense, this paper explores Durkheim’s classical question in 
contemporary terms through analysis of the secular congregation.” 
- 
“SA becomes a site for the enactment of authenticity and encourages mutual reinforcement 
between the self and the secular.” 
- 
“Durkheim would identify as the creative effervescence of the living cult (2001, 323)—can, 
for some, replace the old gods while maintaining the critical social functions, moral 
substance, and normative outcomes of collective religious life. In other words, symbolically 
expressive congregations like the SA can work without belief in a God or the supernatural.” 
- 
Congregations then, be they religious or secular, are an important dimension of 
contemporary social life; namely in that they reveal the enduring relevance of collective 
ritual practice, regardless of the substantive belief systems they express 
- 
The beliefs, values, and motives of Assemblers demonstrate the need of some secular people 
to seek collective “religious” forms that imbue nontheistic worldviews with special import 
through rituals that engage the emotional, moral, and personal. The SA for these 
congregants offers a regular space in which secular beliefs and values find implicit 
legitimation, and for which all things secular can be collectively celebrated, memorialized, 
and made personally compelling. Indeed, the (sub)culturally constructed, collectively 
performed, emotionally suffused embodied ritual practice centered on the secular, is 
precisely what makes Assemblies relevant and powerful to its congregants. 
-

situation, you are welcomed, accepted, and loved.” (Our Mission – Sunday Assembly London, 
n.d.)

B.2 Clustering context factors 
• 
Initial sorting by field. The purpose was to visualize what topics collected context factors 
touched. 
o 
E.g. Spirituality, Urbanization, Project Management, Human Behavior.  
 
• 
Combination, Merging of context factors: 
o 
Several factors reveal another underlying factor : emerging quality cluster 
▪ 
E.g. You can't hide from a megatrend (principle) + People are attached to 
technology (development) + People are living in a fast-paced world (state) = 
Technological innovations are shaping our future 
 
o 
Several factors are summarized into an umbrella-factor: common-quality cluster 
▪ 
E.g. Weather/climate influence peoples mood/culture (state) + Emotional 
response is faster than cognitive (principle) + Dopamine drives motivated 
behavior not actually happiness (principle) = We are unable to control the 
forces of nature and of our own biology 
 
 
 
==PAGE:179==
 
179 
 
B.3 Behaviours needed to feel collectively connected 
We believe that eliciting the following behaviors will have the desired social implications, being 
individuals feeling connectively connected. 
 
• 
To be connected to oneself: Engaging in activities one enjoys, working on oneself 
(psychologically), leaving time for reflection, authentic self-expression 
• 
To be connected with others: Express one’s thoughts and emotions with confidence, being 
present while engaging with others, practice active listening and empathy 
• 
To be connected to the globalized world: Engaging in perceived meaningful activities with 
people from different cultures, displaying a pro-environmental behavior, actively learning 
about the world from non-biased sources. 
 
The lists of aspects of connectedness and behavioral goals are non-exhaustive - we have selected those 
we considered important and that we felt we could initiate with our design intervention. 
These behaviors have then be refined and sorted by their overall desired effects. 4 categories emerged: 
Increased social capital (our main focus), “More meaningful occupations”,” A more conscious 
population with a greater connection to nature” and “Increased presence”.

B.6 Interaction qualities 
B.6.1 Purpose and definition 
The observation was an objective reflection on personal experience and the description of such an 
event from an informal interview. We identified six interaction qualities: solemn, physical, reliable, 
encouraging, playful, and uncomfortable.  
The table below is explaining the interactions through different lenses in four columns: 
• 
Interaction qualities: these are the characteristics we identified to lead to the goal of the 
statement. 
• 
Purpose: it is our intention behind the words we use to describe the interaction qualities (since 
words can carry multiple meanings, we wanted to be explicit about what do we mean) 
• 
Definition: is stating what the dictionary says (this can be different from our meaning) 
• 
Source: is the source of the Definition 
==PAGE:188==
 
188 
 
Statement 
We want people to feel collectively connected while they are engaged in an event.

B.6.2: In-depth explanations 
 
Solemn 
This interaction quality can be the most ambiguous among all. We choose it to express the feeling of 
something festive or celebrational. It is a feeling of special importance which creates a unique 
atmosphere. We believe it will help to create a cohesion between the users. The best way to explain 
it is through the following example. Imagine a wedding celebration after the official ceremony. All 
the guests have gathered to celebrate the married couple. The couple serves as a binding agent in 
this example. They are the ‘design object’ what made all the people gather and celebrate. The guests 
are the ‘users’ who all prepared for the event, physically and mentally. This preparation is not a 
conscious preparation but an unconscious one. It helps in transitioning to the festive, elevated mood 
of the event. It is a different state of mind where and nothing else matters but the married couple 
==PAGE:189==
 
189 
 
and merry celebration. In our case the solemn atmosphere is something meant to aid in achieving 
this transition to a state of mind where people feel connectively connected and present. 
 
Physical 
In our context factors many argued for the importance of physical gatherings and their importance in 
modern society. But it is not only the tribal aspect of the gathering that is so important. Human touch 
and physical contact also have a lot of health benefits. This excludes digital products from our 
solution because it wouldn’t be able to fulfil our goal in the statement. 
 
Reliable 
Reliable is strongly linked with safety. It is important in order to surrender and enjoy the experience 
instead of worrying about risks. Reliability should create the feeling of trust. In a reliable interaction 
the user knows exactly how the system works and what kind of outcome he/she will receive. No 
energy is needed to evaluate risks or consequences so it will help the users to feel present. 
 
Encouraging 
To thrive as an individual or as a group, encouragement in reaching your goals are fundamental. To 
feel connectively connected you also need to encourage people to do it in an explicit or implicit way. 
An encouraging interaction is also supportive, but it gives a positive push, and it helps to overcome 
the doubt and the fear to act on our desires.  
 
Playful 
We believe we all have a desire and a need to play throughout our life. Adults tend to overthink things, 
and many are disconnected from their “inner child”. Overthinking means one only exists in one’s head 
which eventually prevents his/her/their from engaging with his/her/their environment. Playfulness 
helps to bring out our inner child and makes complex tasks into fun exercises. Bringing playfulness into 
the interaction helps one to be more present and more curious. A good example is how easily kids can 
engage with each other. They are more innocent and easier to make friends. We want to think about 
our users as big kids and help them to feel this way so they can lower their boundaries and start natural 
conversations. 
 
Uncomfortable  
This may sound contradictory to what we are trying to make, however the idea behind it is innovative. 
In an interview with us, Harley Dubois argued that putting people in slightly uncomfortable situations 
creates a need to overcome this uncomfortableness through personal effort. This shows the person’s 
dedication and his/her/their investment in the goal, and eventually leads to personal growth. Everyone 
experiencing this discomfort and managing to overcome it alone or together will contribute to feel 
connectively connected.

D: Competitor Analysis and Benchmarking 
 
We mapped out existing concepts in the aera of Copenhagen from the same field, that we defined as 
places, events, or organizations where one would spend time for communing, celebrating, and realizing 
himself. The purpose was to become aware of existing “third places” that would be inspiring and/or 
provoking. If the concept were to be realized in Copenhagen, these could potentially become direct or 
indirect competitors. 
We looked first on the places ability to enable the intended effect (in our case celebration, communing 
and self-realization), and only afterwards what type of place it is (Hekkert & Van Dijk, 2011). The result 
was a broad and heterogenous list of interventions, spanning from bars to collaborative art studios. 
One of our intended effects, realizing oneself is very personal. It could theoretically be anything, big or 
small actions, from making a presentation to your classmates, to opening a café or even be able to talk 
more openly about your feelings to your partner. We chose to focus on activities with cultural, artistic, 
and physical pursuits. Because of our domain, Entertainment with socializing purposes, the selected 
places had to be known for festive events. It can be the main activity (like in a club), more occasional 
and unformal, (like in a culture house), or something happening in a more spontaneous and 
disorganized way (like in a very popular public area). Because of this way of filtering, sports clubs, sport 
facilities as well as most ateliers and public spaces were disregarded. Incubators, scoring high on self-
realization were also disregarded, since they are workspaces and not third places. 
 
Figure 25: Areas studied during the benchmarking, with the scope “places, events or organizations, where one would spend 
time for communing, celebrating and realizing himself” 
 
 
==PAGE:195==
 
195 
 
In total 64 potential competitors were mapped out and sorted according to relevance and category, 
describing shortly what the main functions of each place was. The selection is not exhaustive - the 
purpose wasn’t to identify all places in Copenhagen but to get an overview of what kind of places 
existed in the city. 
Listing of “alternatives” to our concept 
Only the most relevant places were kept, and divided in 2 categories: 
- 
The 15 most relevant candidates were places, with an organization behind, facilitating all three 
activities in an engaging way: communion, celebrations, and possibility to realize oneself 
through art, cultural and/or physical activity. These were defined as our direct competitors. 
 
- 
We considered indirect competitors as places, where people might go in order to realize the 
same goals: communing, celebrating, and realizing oneself – but not intentionally designed for 
these three activities, e.g., Dronning Louises Bro - a bridge between the vibrant neighborhood 
Nørrebro and the city center of Copenhagen - a popular meeting spot for Copenhageners 
where pop-up events of all type occur. Others were chosen because they were places of choice 
for one or two of the activities (celebrating: the high-end venue Chateau Motel, communing 
and realizing oneself: Rört, a movement community offering classes).

For the 5 winners, we used the blue ocean strategy canvas as a tool for verifying the different concepts 
innovativeness, comparing the competitive factors that we believe will become even more relevant 
during the next 10 years. Competitive factors are factors we believe others would compete on with 
Heterotopia. We are conscious that since most of these places are non-profit, they are not “competing 
for a part of the market”. It’s worth noting that we have put these factors together ourselves, so they 
are not part of an obvious industry. Nevertheless, they seem to have common values, which we have 
tried to identify by analyzing their websites, social media channels, combined with additional personal 
experience from the places we have been to.

F: Validation 
F.1: Narrative 
Heterotopia 
In the year 2031, in a middle-sized city of Northern Europe, Josh, who moved to the city 1,5 years ago is 
working remotely as a customer analysis for a drone-sharing app for private use. 
 
Like all employees in Scandinavia, he finishes work at 3 PM - a measure implemented in 2029 to give more 
personal time to workers. But even with more time on his hands, Josh doesn’t feel more happy or fulfilled. 
He is daydreaming about becoming a painter, or at least being able to be creative in his free time. However, 
instead of painting he is usually too drained and unmotivated from an unfulfilling job and finds himself 
binge-watching series in his mindfully designed 30 square meter home LifeHub.   
LifeHub was an architectural project made in collaboration with world-famous interior designers launched 
in 2026 to tackle the housing crisis, where one could pre-order a customized micro-home. 
When Josh finally manages to fall asleep, he is dreaming about being surrounded by people who appreciate 
him. Josh knows very few people in his city, and doesn’t really know any interesting places to go to. He feels 
lonely, sometimes anxious, and lost. 
Through his friend Adam who he hasn't seen for a long time, Josh gets introduced to Heterotopia, a visionary 
social space in the city. Anyone can apply for a membership. This needs to be done in advance in order to 
filter out undesired spontaneous guests showing up randomly. Josh has to fill out a form asking him a little 
bit about himself, his core moral values, why he desires to join, what he wishes to contribute with and what 
he wants to teach and learn (for the Mentoring System). After filling it in, all he can do is to wait until the 
next welcoming ceremony which takes place every second week.  
The Big day arrives! Adam puts on his light fake fur coat in the changing room - Josh brought a hat he never 
dared to wear in public. As Josh walks through the entrance tunnel, a personal message appears on the 
floor. It appears bit by bit as he is walking – “you … are … beau …ti … ful”. It makes him smile. 
==PAGE:210==
 
210 
 
 
 
 
Arriving at the welcoming ceremony, Josh and three other new members are individually warmly greeted 
by their assigned buddy Steve. 
 
The buddy system is in place to aid the members getting introduced, involved, understanding and 
maintaining the culture. 2-3 new members will get a buddy who will act as their main contact person and 
integrator. 
Steve shows him and the others around, introduces them to some of his friends in the Chill Dome, and 
passes by the Conversation Garden before finishing the quick tour at the Atelier. The organised chaos of 
brushes and pigments and the smells the fresh painting fills Josh with joy and inspiration. As Steven notices 
the excitement in Josh’s eyes, he introduces him to Ophelia - a talented painter, who offers to mentor Josh.  
Based on in the Master-apprentice model, The Mentoring System is democratized to all Heterotopians – 
everyone is heavily encouraged to be a teacher and an apprentice. 
The professional support and the amazing atelier gives Josh the boost of motivation he needed to finally 
start on his artistic pursuits. 
==PAGE:211==
 
211 
 
As they move along, Steven ends the tour by explaining the history and the values of the place.  
 
Heterotopia started with a few friends with a vision of creating a meaningful little magical pocket, populated 
with great people, where everyone is encouraged to learn, teach and practice what they truly desire. 
Attracting like-minded people, the community has expanded organically over the last decade, and they are 
now more than 300 members co-owning an old industrial building. The name comes from a concept 
elaborated by Michel Foucault to certain cultural, institutional and discursive spaces that are somehow 
‘other’: disturbing, intense, incompatible, contradictory or transforming. Heterotopias are worlds within 
worlds, mirroring and yet upsetting what is outside. 
 
All this buzz and novelty makes Josh suddenly feel tired, and he naturally seeks out a place to rest and 
disconnect for a while. To his relief, Heterotopia is full of a diverse variety of cosy hangout spots, 
accommodating all types of situations for different moods. He discovers the Moss Pit - a giant sunken sofa 
with a moss-like texture, with small pits unevenly distributed across it- and he lays down in a hollow with a 
view on other people hanging out. The Moss Pit is designed to create spaces for socializing (where pits are 
close to each other) and places for disconnecting (where pits are far from each other). In the evening, 
dimmed warm lights located under the external layer light up in several areas of the sofa, mimicking the 
atmosphere of small fireplaces. Slowly, people naturally gather around these pits of light and Josh finds 
himself surrounded by unfamiliar yet friendly faces. The soothing and reassuring atmosphere makes Josh 
feel relaxed and present, and while conversing he finally feels connected to other people. 
==PAGE:212==
 
212 
 
 
Two months later Josh feels more confident and more in touch with himself. He is now surrounded 
by a community supporting him in his personal interests, most importantly in his artistic pursuits. New 
friends! Josh feels a strong sense of belongingness in this community that collectively takes care of 
everyone. He has gradually replaced his time online at home, with hanging out and engaging in collective 
activities with his friends. He enjoys participating in Heterotopia’s rituals, such as the early Monday Morning 
Party and the frequently held big communal dinners, where people are encouraged to cook food from their 
own culture and share it. Initially introduced by his friend to Heterotopia, he is now himself inviting people 
he think would benefit, just in the same way he did.  
 
 
 
 
By regularly practising painting with Ophelia, he could get through his creative crisis. He is feeling 
revitalized and more aligned with his ideal self. The comfortable and atmospheric chillout places became 
the favourite spots, inviting him to relax and reflect. Besides painting, he signed up to a psychology and 
sleep workshop which gives him a better insight about who he is and starts to work on himself. After 
attending several parties at Heterotopia, Josh gets to enjoy the new electronic music of 2031, especially 
electro-kizomba. He saves songs with his ShazamBracelet - his own idea, that was crowdfunded and 
developed within 3 months, and is now produced on demand inside of Heterotopia. He enjoyed working 
with mechatronics, which made him switch position in his company to a more technical-oriented position. 
==PAGE:213==
 
213 
 
 
Some events are open for the general public, so they can get a feel of what Heterotopia is about. 
After having participated in a public talk by a marine biologist talking about plastic pollution, Josh asks the 
speaker how he can help. He ends up borrowing drones from his company to film polluted areas and 
thereby raising the awareness about the situation. In Heterotopia, many recipients traditionally made of 
plastic are made of a new type of 3d-printed ceramic and are completely biodegradable. 
 
In the future, there will be several Heterotopias in a city and all around the world, following the 
same concept. Current members are encouraged to start or help starting up the same concept in a new 
location. Expanding this way guarantees that the offspring stay true to the vision. The knowledge generated 
inside Heterotopias will be accessible for everyone on an open-source peer-reviewed online library, 
beneficiating society for the greater good.

G: Methodology 
G.1 Use of Designing for Society (Tromp & Hekkert, 2016) 
  
-    Introduction & Chapter 1 
o   Context factors comes from there 
  
-    Effect-driven Design (Chapter 2) 
o   Overall ideology, principles (Find out what exactly) 
o   System thinking: “the place to intervene may not be the place where the problem occurs 
(pp 26-27) 
  
-    A Clash of Concerns (Chapter 3) 
o   Social dilemma theory (p.37) 
o   Strategies to overcome clashes in concern (p.41)         
 
▪ 
Resolve, Bypass, Transform 
  
-    An Imperfect World (Chapter 5) 
Adopting viewpoints from the following sections (pp 62 -65): 
o   The intrusiveness of the commercial world 
o   Limited Willpower 
o   Reinstalling the social 
  
-    Social Implication design: The Method (Chapter 6) 
o   2. Context factors (p 72): look at the domain from a social & user perspective 
o   3. Context structure (p 74): present in an engaging way (ex: infographic) 
o   4. Statement (pp 74-75) 
▪ 
  Goal: to strengthen or discourage behavior (p 75) 
  
-    Tools (in Chapter 7) 
o   Solidifying the statement underlay & laddering (pp 81-83) 
o   Behavioral goal (pp 95 -97) 
  
-    Assessing impact (Chapter 8) 
o   Narrative-based study (pp 100-102) 
  
-    
Inspiration from mentioned case projects
